Run the same kind of analysis on Airbnb, Twitter, Facebook, or nearly any other fast-growing company to come out of Y Combinator, Thiel argues, and the math tells the same story at an even more extreme ratio: something like 85% of the value sits in cash flows expected in 2024 and beyond — more than a decade past the year he gave this lecture. The overwhelming majority of the value sits far in the future, not in this year's growth number. That has an uncomfortable implication for how outsiders judge these companies: growth is the thing everyone can measure right now, so it gets over-weighted. Whether the company will still be around and still be winning in ten years is what actually determines the value, and it's much harder to measure — which is exactly why it gets under-weighted.

The history of value that got captured, and value that didn't

Zoom out further, and the X/Y framing from the start of this chapter explains a genuine pattern across roughly 300 years of technological progress — steam engines, railways, the telephone, refrigeration, household appliances, the computer, aviation — and a closely related 250-year pattern in science: most of it created enormous value and captured almost none.

Science, as an institution, is close to a hundred-percent example of this. The smartest physicist of the twentieth century works out special and general relativity — foundational to enormous amounts of value created afterward — and doesn't become a millionaire, let alone a billionaire, from either discovery. The railroads created enormous value for the societies they connected and mostly went bankrupt doing it, because there were too many competing railroads for any one of them to keep the profit. The Wright brothers flew the first airplane and made essentially no money from having done it. Even the first Industrial Revolution's textile mills, which delivered something like 5–7% efficiency gains every single year for sixty or seventy years — a staggering, compounding run of real technological progress — didn't make the manufacturers who ran them rich either. There were too many of them, competing the gains away as fast as they appeared. Even the landed aristocracy, not the industrialists, still held most of Britain's wealth by 1850.

By Thiel's account, there are really only two categories in that long stretch of history where people captured serious value from genuine innovation, rather than just creating it for everyone else. The first is complex, vertically integrated monopolies — Ford, Standard Oil — where the advantage came not from one single breakthrough but from coordinating an enormous number of moving pieces better than anyone else could. Thiel's modern example is Tesla and SpaceX: neither company's edge, in his account, comes from one single 10x technical breakthrough. It comes from vertical integration done more thoroughly than competitors — Tesla controlling its own distribution instead of ceding margin to dealers the way the rest of the auto industry does, or SpaceX bringing subcontracting in-house instead of leaving itself exposed to suppliers who could otherwise charge monopoly prices on critical parts.

The second category is software, for the structural reasons already covered above: extreme economies of scale, and the ability to reach a global market almost instantly, which lets a small starting market get dominated and expanded from before competitors can catch up. This is, in Thiel's telling, the actual explanation for why Silicon Valley has been so financially successful: not that software people are smarter, but that software as a category is unusually well-suited to building the kind of monopoly this whole chapter has been describing.

Why we're drawn to competition anyway

If avoiding competition is clearly the better outcome, why do so many founders — and whole industries — end up competing instead of monopolizing? Thiel's answer isn't just practical. It's psychological, and he argues it runs deeper than most people are willing to admit.

The word "ape" has meant both "primate" and "to imitate" since at least Shakespeare's time. That's not a coincidence of English vocabulary. It's a hint about human nature: we are, at some level, deeply imitative, and we tend to treat other people wanting the same thing as validation rather than as a warning sign. Thiel's example: roughly twenty thousand people move to Los Angeles every year hoping to become a movie star. About twenty of them make it. Ask any one of those twenty thousand whether so many others chasing the identical goal makes the goal feel more attractive or less, and most say more — proof, in their minds, that the goal must be worth having, since so many people agree.

This gets built into people well before they ever start a company, through an education system that runs almost entirely as a tournament: competing for grades, for college admission, for the right internship, for the right first job. Henry Kissinger's description of his own Harvard faculty is the sharpest version of the same point Thiel has found: "The battles were so ferocious because the stakes were so small." That sounds, on the surface, like a description of people behaving irrationally. Thiel's reading is more specific — when the real differences between competitors are genuinely small, people fight harder, not less hard, to manufacture a difference that feels real.

Thiel offers his own life as the personal version of the same trap. He describes himself as "hypertracked" from early adolescence: a classmate wrote in his eighth-grade yearbook that she knew he'd get into Stanford. Four years later, he did. From there he went to Stanford Law School, and from Stanford Law into a top law firm in New York — exactly the job everyone on the outside of it wanted, and exactly the job everyone already inside it wanted out of. He left after seven months and three days. A colleague told him on his way out, "I had no idea it was possible to escape from Alcatraz" — and then pointed out that the door had been unlocked the whole time. All it had ever taken was walking out the front door and not coming back. So much of that colleague's own identity, and the identities of the people who stayed, had gotten wrapped up in winning a competition that nobody had stopped to ask whether the prize was actually worth having.

None of this means competition doesn't make you better at whatever you're competing at — Thiel is explicit that it does. Comparing yourself against the people around you is a real way to improve. The cost is that it also crowds out a harder, more important question: whether the thing you're getting better at is worth getting better at in the first place. Thiel's closing image is worth carrying past this chapter: don't always go through the tiny door that everyone else is rushing to get through. Go around the corner, and look for the vast gate that nobody else is taking.

⚠️ Common Mistakes

  • Sizing your market by its current, obvious total instead of the smallest slice you can dominate first. This is the same mistake as Chapter 2's "starting at the outer ring," restated here with the clean tech bubble as its most expensive historical example.
  • Describing your market as an "intersection" to sound unique, when it's actually small and fake. ("The only British restaurant in Palo Alto.") If your market description only makes sense with several specific adjectives stacked together, be suspicious of it.
  • Chasing "first mover" status instead of asking whether you'll be the last one standing. Being first is a small, real advantage. Being durable is the one that actually determines value.
  • Treating growth and durability as the same thing. A company can be growing fast and still be in a business that's fundamentally competitive, with no lasting advantage — which means the growth won't hold once competitors catch up.
  • Investing in — or building toward — a single 10x technical breakthrough as the whole strategy, with no plan for what happens once someone else matches it. Disk-drive manufacturers in the 1980s did this repeatedly, improved the world enormously, and made almost no money doing it.

⚖️ Tradeoffs and Edge Cases

Doesn't "last mover" contradict itself — Google and Facebook weren't literally first, so how are they "last"? This is worth resolving directly, because it's a real tension in Thiel's own language. Google wasn't the first search engine — there were several before it. Facebook wasn't the first social network; Reid Hoffman had already started one, called SocialNet, back in 1997. What made each of them "last" wasn't literal chronological order. It was being the first to nail one specific, important dimension well enough that no one after them displaced it: Google was the first to use an automated, algorithmic approach (PageRank) that was an order of magnitude better than manually-curated search. Facebook was the first social network built around real identity rather than invented personas. "Last mover" describes durability along the dimension that actually mattered, not who launched a website first.

Does this chapter's skepticism of "lean startup" methodology contradict the rest of this book's emphasis on talking to users? It looks that way, and it's worth resolving directly rather than leaving the contradiction sitting unaddressed, since Chapters 7, 8, and 16 all lean heavily on user interviews and rapid iteration as core practices. Thiel's actual claim is narrower than "don't talk to users." His own words on this, from the same lecture: founders should spend more effort trying to have "a virtual ESP link with the public" — a direct, almost intuitive read on what people need — and less effort treating iteration itself as the answer. His argument is that the degree of iteration and customer-survey-driven development some founders rely on can become a substitute for genuine insight and differentiation, rather than a source of it. Notice that PayPal and Palantir — his own companies — both started in a deliberately narrow market and both had a specific technical or strategic insight going in; they didn't arrive at their strategy by surveying users into it. The reconciliation: talking to users (the subject of Part Three of this book) is how you refine and validate an idea you already have real conviction about. It's not a substitute for having that conviction in the first place. A founder with no differentiated insight who iterates rapidly on user feedback can still end up with a well-executed, undifferentiated product — competing, not monopolizing.

Is Thiel's four-characteristics model the only way to build a durable business? No — Thiel is explicit that there's no single formula, and that the vertically-integrated model (Ford, Tesla, SpaceX) doesn't fit neatly into "proprietary technology, network effects, economies of scale, branding" at all. It's really a fifth path: winning through superior coordination of many ordinary pieces, rather than through one exceptional piece. This matters if your business involves physical operations, logistics, or manufacturing, where the software-style economies of scale don't automatically apply.

🧪 Applying the Test: Is This a Monopoly, or a Competitive Business Wearing a Costume?

Take an idea, and try describing its market honestly in both directions.

First, describe it the way a struggling competitor would describe theirs: as narrowly and specifically as you can, stacking on adjectives until it sounds unique. If that description only sounds meaningful because of how many qualifiers you had to add, you've probably found an "intersection" lie rather than a real, defensible position.

Second, describe it the way an actual monopoly downplays itself: as the union of several much larger categories, so the real dominant position gets lost in a crowd of imagined competitors. If your honest internal sense of the business — the one you'd only say out loud to a cofounder — sounds a lot closer to the second description than the first, you may actually be closer to a real monopoly than your public pitch admits.

The test that matters, per Thiel's own answer to this exact question in a Q&A after his lecture: ignore the narrative in both directions, and ask what the actual market is — not the story that makes you sound uniquely interesting, and not the story that makes you sound reassuringly unthreatening. People, in his words, "have incentives to powerfully distort these things," in both directions at once, and the only way through is to keep asking what's real underneath the story.

✅ Chapter Summary

  • A company's value depends on two independent things: how much value it creates (X) and what percentage of that value it captures (Y). Being big (a large X) doesn't automatically mean being valuable (a large X times Y).
  • There are really only two kinds of businesses: monopolies, which are rare, stable, and profitable, and competitive businesses, where almost nobody makes money. There is very little in between.
  • Monopolies downplay themselves to avoid regulators; competitive businesses inflate themselves to attract investors. The lies run in opposite directions, which is why the difference is hard to see from the outside.
  • The path to monopoly starts small — a market so tiny it looks worthless on a spreadsheet — and expands outward in concentric rings, the same structure Chapter 2 introduced from a completely different lecture.
  • Durable monopolies usually have at least one of: proprietary technology roughly 10x better than the alternative, network effects, extreme economies of scale, or branding — and software is unusually well-suited to several of these at once.
  • Aim to be the last mover in a category, not the first — durability, not speed to market, is what determines whether most of a company's value (often concentrated a decade or more in the future) actually gets realized.
  • Across 250 years of innovation, most value created was never captured by the people who created it — science, railroads, and early industrial manufacturing all show this. The rare exceptions are complex vertically-integrated businesses and software.

📝 Exercises

  1. Recall: In your own words, explain why a monopoly and a struggling competitive business would describe their markets in exactly opposite ways, even though both are lying.
  2. Apply: Take an idea you're evaluating. Write its market description twice — once the way a monopoly would understate it, and once the way a weak competitor would overstate it. Which one is closer to true?
  3. Apply: Using the characteristics table in this chapter, identify which of proprietary technology, network effects, economies of scale, or branding your idea could plausibly have. If the honest answer is "none yet," what would need to be true in a year for that to change?
  4. Confront a counterexample: This chapter argues that heavy reliance on user surveys and rapid iteration can substitute for genuine differentiated insight rather than produce it. But Part Three of this book (particularly Chapters 7 and 8) treats direct user feedback as close to essential. Reconcile these two claims in your own words — what is each one actually arguing, and where exactly is the line between them?

📚 Further Reading

  • Peter Thiel's book Zero to One, co-written with Blake Masters, expands the entire argument of this chapter — especially the X/Y framing and the last-mover argument — well beyond what a single lecture could cover.
  • Warren Buffett's shareholder letters are the original source of the "moat" terminology this chapter introduces, applied originally to public-market investing rather than startups.
  • Chapter 2 of this book covers the concentric-expansion structure from Sam Altman's independent version of the same argument — worth rereading side by side with this chapter's version from Thiel.
🤝

Chapter 4 — Choosing a Cofounder

One Y Combinator batch, Sam Altman recalls, had about seventy-five companies. Nine of them added a new cofounder sometime between their interview and the actual start of the program — someone who joined the team late, after the selection process was already done. All nine of those nine companies fell apart within the following year.

Not most of them. All of them.

That number should reframe how you think about this decision. Most first-time founders treat choosing a cofounder as something close to a social accident — you meet someone who also wants to start a company, you both seem into it, you start a company together. Altman's blunt assessment of this pattern: "You would never hire someone like this, and yet people are willing to choose their business partners this way." This chapter is about why that casualness is dangerous, and what to actually look for instead.

💡 The Core Idea: You Can Trust Your Instincts About People

Chapter 1 made a point that's worth restating precisely, because this chapter is about to say something that sounds like its opposite: you cannot reliably know in advance whether you are capable of building a startup. Confidence going in doesn't predict outcomes.

Paul Graham makes a related but distinct claim about other people, and it points the opposite direction: your instincts about people are actually trustworthy, more trustworthy than most founders give them credit for. His observation, from watching founders make this mistake repeatedly: they meet someone who seems impressive, feel some private unease about them anyway, and override that unease because the person's credentials or confidence are hard to argue with. Then, months later, when the relationship falls apart, they say some version of: "I knew there was something wrong about that guy, but I ignored it because he seemed so impressive."

Graham's advice is almost embarrassingly simple: pick a cofounder the way you'd pick a friend. Not a colleague, not a business contact — a friend. Work with someone you would generally like and respect, and whom you've known long enough to actually be sure of, because plenty of people are extremely good at seeming likable for a while. The real test only arrives once your interests are in conflict, which is exactly the situation a new startup manufactures constantly.

These two claims — don't trust your self-assessment (Chapter 1), do trust your read on other people (this chapter) — aren't in tension. They're about different kinds of uncertainty. What you're capable of becoming is genuinely unknowable in advance, because starting a company changes you in ways you can't simulate ahead of time. Who someone already is — whether they're trustworthy, whether they follow through, whether you'd actually want to be around them during a crisis — is much more visible, if you're willing to look honestly instead of being dazzled.

🔍 Why It's True: The Data, the Metaphor, and the Wrong Way to Meet Someone

The choice matters more than almost any other you'll make

Altman is direct about the stakes: cofounder relationships are among the most important in the entire company, and cofounder blowups are Y Combinator's single most common cause of early startup death. That's a strong claim to make about one relationship, in a book full of chapters about products, growth, and fundraising — but the data backs it up. Looking across the most valuable companies YC has funded, almost all of them have at least two founders. Y Combinator funds solo founders at roughly a tenth the rate of teams. The ranking, in Altman's own words, goes: a cofounder you already know well is best. A solo founder is acceptable — worse, but survivable. A cofounder you barely know, chosen the way many students choose one, is the worst option on the list, worse than having no cofounder at all.

That last point is worth sitting with, because it cuts against instinct. It feels safer to have a partner than to go alone. The data says a bad partner is more dangerous than no partner.

Reid Hoffman, who co-founded LinkedIn and has invested in dozens of startups since, frames the reason teams beat solo founders in a way worth stating directly: it explodes the myth of the "super founder." Popular imagination pictures a founder — the Steve Jobs, Bill Gates, Elon Musk, Mark Zuckerberg, Jeff Bezos image — as someone who can do everything — product, fundraising, sales, management, strategy — at a world-class level, alone: a kind of panopticon of skills, watching and excelling at every function of the company at once. Hoffman is candid that he once bought into this image himself, recalling an article early in his own career that called Bill Gates "smarter than Einstein" — a claim, he points out, that Gates himself probably wouldn't want standing next to his name, and a symptom of exactly this fantasy: the founder as someone who can jump over tall buildings in a single bound. In reality, nobody has that full range of skills, and a company that depends on one person having it is fragile in a way that isn't obvious until something goes wrong in the exact area that person is weak. Two or three founders, chosen well, let you cover each other's gaps and split a genuinely superhuman set of demands across real, ordinary humans. This is also, not coincidentally, the same reason Chapter 1 spent so much time debunking the myth of the founder as a lone, glamorous decision-maker — this chapter's version of that myth is simply the same fantasy applied to a team of one instead of a team of several.

The James Bond test

Y Combinator has an internal phrase for the kind of cofounder you want: "relentlessly resourceful." Paul Graham popularized a more memorable, more specific version of the same idea at YC's own orientation sessions, and Altman has kept using it since: you're looking for someone who behaves like James Bond.

Not because you need an expert marksman. Because the archetype captures a specific cluster of traits better than a checklist would: unflappable under pressure, tough, knows what to do in almost any situation, decisive, creative, ready for anything. Altman's framing: you need someone who behaves this way more than you need someone who happens to be a deep expert in one particular domain. Domain expertise can be learned or hired in. Being unflappable under real pressure mostly can't.

Alongside "relentlessly resourceful," a few more specific traits matter: tough, and calm — especially if you know you aren't naturally either of those things yourself. Smart is assumed; almost everyone already screens for that and undervalues the rest. And if you're not a technical person, you want a technical cofounder, particularly if you're building a software company. Altman is skeptical of the increasingly popular idea that a non-technical founding team can just "hire the technical people" and focus on being great managers instead: "Software people should really be starting software companies."

How many, and how close

Y Combinator's experience points to two or three cofounders as close to the ideal number. One founder, working alone, is workable but harder. Five is close to unworkable — too many voices, too much coordination overhead before the company has any process to manage it. Four sometimes works. Two or three is the real target.

Getting to know a potential cofounder takes time — ideally years, not weeks. This is part of why "cofounder dating" events, where strangers meet and agree to start a company within the same afternoon, are close to the worst possible way to find one. Altman's advice, matter-of-factly: take advantage of whatever environment already puts you around smart, ambitious people for an extended period — college is one version of this, working at a company full of people like Facebook or Google's early employees is another. Being in either environment for years is, in a very literal sense, cofounder-rich in a way that a one-off networking event never will be.

A modern illustration of what "already knowing someone" looks like at its most extreme, outside anything covered in the original lectures: Patrick and John Collison, who founded Stripe, are brothers. That's obviously not a formula anyone can copy — you can't manufacture a sibling — but the underlying mechanism is exactly the one this section has been describing. By the time they started a company together, there was no discovery process left to do. They already knew, in complete and tested detail, how the other person behaved under real pressure, what they were actually good at, and whether they could trust each other completely. Most founders have to build that knowledge deliberately, over years of working or studying next to someone. The Collisons simply arrived at the starting line already having it.

Being in the same place

One of Altman's more absolute claims, delivered without much hedging: don't have cofounders working from different locations. His reasoning is that in the early days of a startup, speed and constant, informal communication outweigh nearly everything else, and video calls — even good ones — don't replicate that. His supporting evidence: look at a list of the most successful software companies of all time and try to find one where the founders were based in different cities from day one. He couldn't find a clean example, and treated the absence as close to conclusive.

Sidebar: What's Changed Since 2014

This is one of the few claims in this book that later evidence has genuinely complicated. GitLab was built from the start as an entirely remote company and went public in 2021 with no central office at all. Zapier's founders were geographically distributed from early on and built a large, durable business that way. Neither company disproves Altman's underlying point about the value of fast, informal, high-bandwidth communication — both invested heavily and deliberately in written culture, asynchronous processes, and tooling specifically to compensate for not sharing a room. What's changed since 2014 is less "remote cofounders can work" (a few examples always existed) and more that an entire toolkit for making it work — better video infrastructure, a cultural expectation of async-first communication, a labor market used to it — has matured enough that the deliberate substitution is easier to pull off than it was when this lecture was given. The underlying warning still holds: if you go remote, it has to be a deliberate design choice with real infrastructure behind it, not a default you fell into because it seemed easier to start that way.

Reid Hoffman, teaching a related lecture in the same course, makes a companion point about location that's easy to conflate with Altman's but is actually distinct: it's not that Silicon Valley is unconditionally the right place to start every company. It's that different businesses need different networks, and Silicon Valley is unusually good at supplying the network some specific kinds of businesses need — software, mobile, marketplaces — and much less good at supplying what other businesses need. His example is Groupon, which required an enormous, old-fashioned sales force as a core part of its early growth strategy. That kind of plan — rent a large office, staff multiple floors with salespeople — tends to get criticized rather than funded in Silicon Valley, where investors and talent are oriented around capital efficiency and network effects instead. Groupon started in Chicago, where that model wasn't a strange or unfundable idea. Hoffman's broader point: ask what kind of company you're actually building, and go to wherever the right network for that kind of company already exists — which is usually Silicon Valley for the categories this book focuses on, but isn't automatically true for every category of business.

Decide the equity split early, and make it near-equal

There's one conversation founders reliably put off, for no good reason: how to split the equity. Altman has watched founders delay this for months, sometimes structuring their own incorporation paperwork in strange ways specifically to avoid having the conversation. His observation: this isn't a discussion that gets easier with time. It gets harder, because the longer you wait, the more each person's sense of what they've already contributed hardens into a position. The right time to have it is near the very start — ideally within the first few weeks of deciding to work together, before the company has gotten far enough along for the conversation to feel like it's litigating the past.

The split itself should be close to equal. This isn't just a fairness norm — it's a diagnostic. If you find yourself unwilling to give a prospective cofounder a genuinely equal share, Altman's advice is to treat that reluctance as information: it's a sign you should think hard about whether you actually want them as a cofounder at all, rather than a sign you should negotiate them down. A cofounder relationship you're already structuring to protect yourself from is a relationship with a problem in it from day one.

None of this replaces the legal mechanism that actually protects the company if the relationship falls apart later — vesting, which Chapter 15 covers in full. This section is about the decision that vesting later locks in, not the paperwork itself: decide early, decide close to equal, and treat any resistance to doing either as a signal worth listening to.

⚠️ Common Mistakes

  • Treating cofounder selection with less rigor than hiring an employee. You would never hire someone you'd known for an afternoon into your first ten employees. Doing the equivalent with a cofounder — someone who will own a much larger share of the company and be much harder to remove — is a worse version of the same mistake.
  • Meeting a cofounder through a "cofounder dating" event or a cold introduction and starting immediately. The track record for teams that don't already know each other well is poor, and it doesn't improve much even when both people seem genuinely talented individually.
  • Ignoring a private sense of unease about someone because they seem impressive. This is exactly the mistake Paul Graham describes founders making again and again — and by the time it's confirmed, the equity and the company structure are already built around the person.
  • Building a solo-founder company by default, rather than by genuine necessity. A solo founder can succeed, but the data suggests it's a real, measurable handicap — worth accepting only if the alternative is a bad cofounder, not as a first choice.
  • Choosing a location based on where you happen to be, rather than where the network your specific business needs actually exists.

⚖️ Tradeoffs and Edge Cases

Doesn't this chapter's "trust your instincts about people" contradict Chapter 1's "you can't tell if you're capable"? No, but it's worth stating clearly why not, since they sound similar on the surface. Chapter 1's claim is about self-knowledge under a kind of change you haven't experienced yet — you can't know who you'll become once a startup takes over your life, because nothing in most people's prior experience simulates that. This chapter's claim is about reading someone else's existing character: whether they follow through, whether they're honest under pressure, whether you'd trust them when it's hard. That's a different kind of judgment, built from ordinary life experience picking friends and colleagues, and Graham's argument is that this ordinary judgment transfers just fine — the mistake founders make isn't lacking the instinct, it's overriding the instinct they already have.

Are there real exceptions to "never have remote cofounders"? Yes, and the sidebar above covers the clearest ones (GitLab, Zapier). The honest synthesis: the underlying reason for Altman's advice — fast, high-bandwidth, informal communication matters more in the first year than almost anything else — hasn't gone away. What's changed is that it's now possible to build that bandwidth deliberately without sharing a room, if you invest in it as seriously as those companies did. Choosing to be remote by default because it's convenient, without that investment, is still exactly the trap Altman is warning against.

Is a solo founder ever the right call? Occasionally — some of the highest-valued YC companies have had solo founders, and Altman doesn't claim it's impossible, only that the data shows it's harder. The honest framing from this chapter and the last one combined: a solo founder who is genuinely compelled (Chapter 1's test) and genuinely capable of covering both the technical and non-technical demands of the business is a real, survivable path. It's a worse bet than a great cofounder team, and a much better bet than a mediocre one.

🧪 Applying the Test

Think about a specific person you've considered starting a company with. Run Graham's test directly: if you strip away their resume, their confidence, and how impressive they seemed the first time you met them, would you still describe them the way you'd describe a genuine friend — someone you like, respect, and have actually seen operate under some kind of pressure? Not "someone impressive I'd enjoy being associated with." Someone whose character you'd vouch for even if their startup failed and they had nothing to show for it.

Then run the reference-check version of the same test, the way you would for a serious hire rather than a friend: ask someone who has actually worked with them, closely, under stress, whether they were still the same person once things got hard. If the honest answer to either question is uncertain, the data in this chapter says that uncertainty is itself the answer — not a detail to resolve after you've already started the company together.

✅ Chapter Summary

  • Cofounder blowups are Y Combinator's most common cause of early startup death — in one batch, all nine companies that added a late, unfamiliar cofounder failed within a year.
  • You can't reliably judge your own future capability (Chapter 1), but you can and should trust your instincts about another person's character — the mistake is overriding that instinct because someone seems impressive.
  • The ranking that matters: a cofounder you already know well, then a solo founder, then — worst of all — a cofounder you barely know.
  • "Relentlessly resourceful," embodied by the James Bond archetype, matters more than deep domain expertise in a cofounder; domain expertise can be hired in later.
  • Two or three cofounders is the practical target. Getting there usually takes years of actually knowing someone, not a single event designed to pair up strangers.
  • Being in the same physical location remains the default advice, though remote-first companies since 2014 (GitLab, Zapier) show it can work with serious, deliberate investment in communication — not as a default.
  • The right location for a company depends on which network that specific business needs, not just where Silicon Valley happens to be strong.
  • Decide the equity split early and keep it near-equal. Reluctance to give a cofounder an equal share is itself useful information about whether you actually want them as a cofounder.

📝 Exercises

  1. Recall: Explain, in your own words, why "trust your instincts about people" isn't a contradiction of Chapter 1's claim that you can't reliably judge your own future capability.
  2. Apply: Think of someone you've considered as a cofounder. Run the reference-check test from this chapter's "Applying the Test" section on them honestly. What did you learn that you hadn't consciously admitted to yourself before?
  3. Apply: If you don't currently have a cofounder, identify one environment in your life right now (school, work, a community) that's genuinely cofounder-rich in the way this chapter describes. What would it take to actually spend real time there, rather than treating cofounder-finding as a one-off event?
  4. Confront a counterexample: GitLab and Zapier both succeeded as deliberately remote companies, directly against Altman's advice in this chapter. What specifically would you need to see in your own founding team's plan to know whether you're doing what they did — building real infrastructure for remote collaboration — versus just defaulting to remote because it's easier to start that way?

📚 Further Reading

  • Paul Graham's own CS183B lecture is the direct source of the "trust your instincts about people" argument this chapter builds on.
  • Reid Hoffman's own CS183B lecture, only briefly drawn on in this chapter for the team-composition and location material, covers a different set of topics at greater length in Chapter 18 of this book (the investment thesis, and navigating a founder's recurring paradoxes) — not LinkedIn's early hiring, which isn't repeated there.
  • Chapter 15 covers the mechanics of cofounder vesting in full — the legal structure that protects a company if a cofounder relationship does fall apart.
🧑‍💼

Chapter 5 — The First Ten Hires

Airbnb spent five months interviewing its first employee. In the company's entire first year, it hired two people. Before any of that, Brian Chesky and his cofounders wrote down the culture values they wanted every future Airbnb employee to hold — before there was a product worth using, before there was any revenue, before there was anyone to apply the values to yet. One of those values was that you had to "bleed Airbnb." Chesky used to ask candidates whether they'd take the job if they'd just been told they had one year left to live. He later decided that was a little too intense and relaxed it to ten years. As far as anyone at Y Combinator has heard, he still asks it.

This is not how most first-time founders hire. Most first-time founders hire the way they'd hire for anything else that felt urgent: post a listing, take some interviews, extend an offer to the strongest candidate within a reasonable amount of time. This chapter is about why that instinct, applied to a startup's first ten employees, is one of the more expensive mistakes a founder can make — and what the alternative actually looks like.

💡 The Core Idea: Try Not To Hire, Then Hire Completely Differently

The starting stance, before any hiring tactics matter at all, is this: try not to hire. Sam Altman's observation about the psychology of headcount is worth stating plainly, because it cuts against a natural instinct — people treat a company's employee count as a proxy for how real and impressive it is. Say a high number, and people are impressed. Say a low number, and it can feel like an admission that the company isn't serious yet. The actual relationship runs the other way. A large headcount usually means a high burn rate, more complexity, and slower decisions. The startups that stay smallest longest, for their size and stage, are frequently the ones doing the best — proud of how much they can get done with how few people, not embarrassed by it.

That said, at some point you do have to hire — and once you're in hiring mode, the rule changes completely. Altman's answer to how much time hiring should take: zero percent, or twenty-five percent. There's no comfortable middle. Either you're not hiring at all right now, or it is close to the single largest block of your time as a founder. Most management books recommend something like fifty percent, but Altman notes that the people who give that advice rarely spend even ten percent of their own time on it themselves. Twenty-five percent, done for real, is already an enormous commitment — and it's what's actually required to get this right.

The reason this matters so much comes down to one blunt fact: the cost of getting an early hire wrong is far higher than the cost of getting a later one wrong. A mediocre hire at a company with a thousand employees causes real but contained damage — that person is one of a thousand, and the company's culture and standards absorb the mistake. A mediocre hire among a company's first five or ten people sets the tone for everyone who joins after them, and Altman has seen it kill companies outright. A friend of his keeps a sign in his conference room, angled so interview candidates can read it during the interview: mediocre engineers do not build great companies. It's blunt, and it's true, and it's exactly the standard a big company can survive violating in a way a ten-person startup can't.

🔍 Why It's True: Culture Before Hiring, Sourcing Beyond LinkedIn, and What to Actually Test For

Write the culture down before you hire anyone

Airbnb's approach — writing culture values down before making a single hire — sounds like a strange amount of process for a company with no employees yet. Patrick Collison of Stripe, teaching later in the same course, offers the underlying logic for why it isn't overkill: when you hire your first ten people, you're not really hiring ten people. You're hiring something closer to a hundred, because each of those first ten will go on to bring in roughly ten more people who resemble them culturally. Deciding, deliberately, what those first ten should be like — before you're under pressure to just fill a role — is one of the highest-leverage decisions a founder makes, precisely because its effects compound for years afterward.

At Airbnb, this showed up concretely: every one of the company's first forty or so employees reports feeling like they were part of founding the company, not like they joined after the fact. That feeling doesn't happen by accident. It happens because the company set an extremely high bar — Airbnb spent five months on its first hire alone — and everyone who cleared that bar knew, going in, exactly what they were signing up to believe in.

There is no wrong place to find people, but there is a best one

Personal referrals — people you already know, and people your existing employees already know — are, by a wide margin, the best source of early hires. Most founders feel awkward calling every talented person they've ever met and asking their own employees to do the same. Do it anyway: at a large company like Facebook or Google, one of the first things that happens to a new hire is an HR conversation designed specifically to extract every talented person they know, for exactly this reason. A useful, underused variant: look outside the immediate geographic area you're hiring in. Competition for engineers in a place like Silicon Valley is brutal; the person you already know from somewhere else in the world may be just as good and much easier to bring on.

Ben Silbermann, Pinterest's cofounder, makes a complementary point from the other direction: there's no wrong place to look, even if referrals are the best source on average. His own early hiring included posting ads on Craigslist, showing up to random tech talks, and running weekly bring-your-own-food-and-drink barbecues at the office just to get people talking. His candid observation about where a lot of his best people actually turned up: any time he went to get coffee at a nearby café, there'd be another founder there doing exactly the same kind of recruiting. Genuinely good people are almost always doing something else already — you have to go find them, especially when nobody has heard of your company yet.

That last point — nobody's heard of you yet — is worth sitting with, because it's the central obstacle to hiring anyone at all in the first few months. John Collison describes what this actually feels like: you're two people working on something strange that nobody recognizes, trying to convince someone to leave a stable situation for it. For Stripe's second hire, his own friends staged what Collison calls "a full-on assault" the night before he accepted, trying to talk him out of joining. He joined anyway. One of those same friends who tried to talk him out of it later joined Stripe himself.

Given that friction, a specific and slightly counterintuitive sourcing insight from Patrick Collison is worth naming directly: look for people who are early in their careers or currently undervalued by the broader market, rather than trying to poach someone already recognized as excellent. Someone the market has already correctly identified as a "known spectacular quantity" is, by definition, already happy somewhere and hard to move. Stripe's early team included a designer who was eighteen and still in high school in Sweden, and a future CTO who was still in college. Neither looked, on paper, like an obvious hire for a payments company. Both turned out to be exactly the kind of person the company needed — because the company was betting on who they'd become, not just who they already were on a resume.

What to actually test for, and how

Once you have candidates, the traits that matter are more specific than "smart" — smart is close to a given, and most founders already screen for it without being told to. Altman's own three-question test, applied consistently across every hire he's ever made and rarely regretted: is this person smart? Do they get things done? Do I want to spend a lot of time around them? Answer yes to all three, and it's almost always worked out.

Patrick and John Collison arrived independently at a closely related, slightly more specific set of three traits after examining what their own first ten hires had in common: they were genuine — people others trusted and wanted to work with; they cared a great deal, to a degree Collison himself now considers a little extreme in hindsight (every API error at Stripe was, for a period, emailed directly to the founders' personal inboxes, and early employees would copy each other on outgoing emails just to flag small grammar mistakes); and they were people who finished things, as opposed to people who were excited about many projects but rarely completed any of them.

The best way to actually assess any of this isn't a standard interview. It's working together on something real, even briefly, before formally hiring. If you've already worked with someone, in many cases you don't need a traditional interview at all — you already have the evidence. If you haven't, a short paid project, ideally spanning a couple of days, teaches both sides more than any conversation could. Where an interview is unavoidable, ask about specific past projects in detail rather than reaching for brainteasers — a pattern Altman notes young technical cofounders fall into surprisingly often, apparently because it feels more rigorous than it actually is.

References are the step first-time founders skip most often, and the step that matters most when it isn't skipped. The wrong way to reference-check is to ask "how was so-and-so?" and accept a pleasant, generic answer. The right way, borrowed from Ben Silbermann's own practice: force the reference to be specific by asking a version of "if I asked [a mutual contact] what you're best at, or what you're working to improve, what would they say?" — which builds in social accountability the reference can't easily dodge. Then push further: is this person in the top five percent of everyone you've worked with? The top ten? Would you hire them again? If not, why not? A generic answer to "were they good?" tells you almost nothing. A forced ranking against everyone else that person has worked with tells you a great deal.

One more correlated signal worth tracking, per Altman's own observation across many YC companies: communication skills predict whether a hire works out more reliably than founders initially expect. Someone who struggles to explain themselves clearly in an interview tends to struggle the same way once they're actually on the team, under real pressure. One last practical heuristic, from Mark Zuckerberg by way of Altman: would you be comfortable hanging out with this person socially, and would you be comfortable reporting to them if your roles were reversed? You don't need to be friends with everyone you hire, but if the honest answer to both is no, that's worth taking seriously.

Give employees real equity — more than feels natural

Founders consistently make an equity mistake in a specific, predictable direction: generous with investors, stingy with employees. Altman's advice is to reverse that instinct. His own rough benchmark: aim to give about ten percent of the company, combined, to your first ten employees. They'll earn it over the standard four-year vesting period, and if they're genuinely good, they'll go on to create far more value than that ten percent represents. Investors write a check once and, despite the promises that often come with a term sheet, frequently contribute less ongoing value than founders expect. Employees are the ones who build the company, year after year. The YC companies that have been unusually generous with early equity are, in Altman's account, disproportionately represented among the ones that went on to be the most successful.

Keep the people you just spent months hiring

Hiring well and then managing badly wastes the whole effort. A specific, recurring failure mode among first-time founders: telling people when they're doing something wrong constantly, because it feels obviously correct to point out mistakes as you see them, without realizing that a team told they're failing every day will simply leave. The founders who get this right tend to follow a few unglamorous habits: let the team take public credit for what goes well, and take the blame yourself for what goes badly. Don't micromanage — hand people real, if initially small, areas of responsibility, and let that responsibility grow. Researcher Dan Pink's framework for what actually motivates people to do their best work — autonomy, mastery, and purpose — is worth taking seriously even though it's rarely top of mind for a first-time founder buried in product work. Regular one-on-ones and direct, specific feedback round out the list. None of this is complicated. All of it is easy to skip when you're moving fast, which is exactly why it gets skipped.

Fire fast when it isn't working

The flip side of generous hiring is fast firing, and first-time founders reliably get this wrong in the same direction: they wait too long, hoping the person will turn it around. The better rule, painful as it is in the moment, is to fire quickly once it's clear something isn't working — it's better for the company, and it's usually better for the person too, who's stuck in a role they're failing at. Two specific patterns are worth watching for beyond simple underperformance: people who create office politics, and people who are persistently negative, since both are corrosive to a small team in a way a large company can better absorb. The test for knowing it's genuinely time to act, rather than just a rough patch: everyone makes mistakes occasionally, and that's normal — the signal to actually act is when someone is getting nearly every decision wrong, consistently, over weeks or a month, to the point where it's obvious to everyone on the team, not just to you.

⚠️ Common Mistakes

  • Hiring to look more legitimate, rather than because the work genuinely requires it. A low headcount is a sign of efficiency, not a sign of being unserious — treat it that way.
  • Interviewing with brainteasers instead of asking about real past projects. Past work is a far better predictor of future work than a clever puzzle solved under artificial pressure.
  • Accepting a vague, pleasant answer from a reference instead of forcing specificity. "They were great to work with" is not a data point. "Top five percent of everyone you've worked with, and here's specifically why" is.
  • Compromising on a hire because of a pressing deadline. Every founder does this eventually as the company scales, and it's regretted almost every time it happens among the first five to ten hires specifically.
  • Only sourcing from people who already look impressive on paper. The most valuable early hires are frequently undervalued by the broader market — early in their careers, or overlooked for reasons that have nothing to do with how good they actually are.
  • Skipping the culture-values conversation until the company already has employees. By the time you need the values to resolve a real disagreement, it's too late to invent them from scratch.
  • Being generous with equity to investors and stingy with employees. It's usually backwards — employees create most of a company's long-term value.
  • Telling people they're doing something wrong constantly, without also giving them credit and real ownership. This is one of the fastest ways to cause otherwise-good hires to quit.
  • Waiting too long to fire someone who is clearly, consistently failing. It's painful in the moment and usually better for both the company and the person once it finally happens.

⚖️ Tradeoffs and Edge Cases

Does "try not to hire" ever stop applying? Yes — this chapter's advice is specifically about the earliest hires, where the cost of a mistake is highest and the company has the least infrastructure to absorb one. Later in a company's life, the opposite skill becomes necessary: hiring fast and scaling a team deliberately. Chapter 21 covers what changes once a company crosses roughly twenty-five employees and needs real management structure for the first time — the "try not to hire" instinct from this chapter is explicitly not the right instinct once you're there.

Does experience matter, or doesn't it? Both, depending on the role. For a role running a large, established part of the organization, real experience usually matters. For most early hires at a very young startup, Altman's own experience is that aptitude and genuine belief in the mission matter more than having done the exact job before — some of the best hires he's ever been involved in had never done the specific role they were hired into. The judgment call is knowing which kind of role you're filling, and not defaulting to "more experience is always better" without asking that question first.

What happens when someone who was a great early hire stops being able to grow with the company? This is a real, common problem — someone hired to lead engineering at five people may not be the right person to lead a team of fifty. Chapter 19 covers a more precise framework (task-relevant maturity) for reasoning about when someone can grow into a bigger role versus when a role needs to change around them. The short version for this chapter: genuinely smart, adaptable people can usually find some valuable role as the company scales, even if it isn't the one they started in — which is a reason to hire for underlying aptitude in the first place, not just for the specific job description in front of you today.

🧪 Applying the Test

Take someone you're considering hiring, or someone already on your team whose fit you're unsure about. Don't ask yourself the vague version of the question — "are they good?" — because it's too easy to answer yes by default. Ask the forced version instead: if you called three people who've worked closely with this person, would each of them independently rank them in the top five percent of everyone they've worked with? Would each of them tell a specific story about something this person finished, under pressure, without being managed closely? If you're not confident what the honest answer would be, that uncertainty is itself the signal — the same way it was in Chapter 4's cofounder test. The fix isn't to hope it resolves itself. It's to go make the calls before you decide, not after.

✅ Chapter Summary

  • Try not to hire until you genuinely need to — a small headcount is a sign of efficiency, not a sign of being unserious.
  • Once you are hiring, it should take zero percent of your time or roughly twenty-five percent — there's no comfortable middle, because getting it right requires real, sustained attention.
  • A bad hire among your first five to ten people can kill the company outright, in a way a bad hire at a thousand-person company can't.
  • Write your culture values down before you hire anyone, the way Airbnb did — deciding this deliberately, rather than under pressure to fill a role, compounds for years.
  • Personal referrals are the best source of candidates, but there's no wrong place to look — Ben Silbermann hired through Craigslist, tech talks, and office barbecues.
  • Test for specific, concrete traits — smart, gets things done, someone you want to spend time with; or Stripe's version: genuine, cares a great deal, finishes things — rather than generic intelligence alone.
  • Work together on something real before formally hiring, whenever possible. When you can't, ask about specific past projects instead of brainteasers, and force specificity out of every reference check.
  • The most valuable early hires are frequently undervalued by the broader market — early in their careers, not yet recognized as exceptional by anyone else.
  • Be generous with equity toward employees, not just investors — roughly ten percent of the company to the first ten hires is a reasonable benchmark.
  • Retention is a management habit, not a one-time decision: give credit publicly, take blame yourself, avoid micromanaging, and don't tell people they're failing every day.
  • Fire fast once it's clearly not working — waiting is worse for the company and usually worse for the person, too.

📝 Exercises

  1. Recall: Explain why the cost of a bad hire is so much higher in a company's first ten employees than in its thousandth.
  2. Apply: Write down your own company's (real or hypothetical) culture values, the way Airbnb did before hiring anyone. If you already have employees, would each of your first few hires have clearly passed those values, or were the values invented after the fact to describe people you'd already hired?
  3. Apply: Pick someone currently on a team you're part of, or someone you're considering hiring. Draft the specific reference-check questions from this chapter you'd actually ask about them, and predict honestly what the answers would be.
  4. Confront a counterexample: This chapter argues aptitude and mission-belief matter more than experience for most early hires. But some of the companies covered elsewhere in this book (enterprise software, capital-intensive businesses) clearly do need specific deep expertise from day one. How would you tell, for your own company, which category you're in?

📚 Further Reading

  • Patrick and John Collison's account of Stripe's early hiring, referenced throughout this chapter, is covered from the culture-building side in Chapter 6.
  • Chapter 19 covers task-relevant maturity — the framework for deciding how much a hire can grow into a larger role as the company scales.
  • Chapter 21 covers what changes about hiring once a company crosses roughly twenty-five employees and "try not to hire" stops being the right default.
🎨

Chapter 6 — Designing Company Culture

In the fall of 2008, Airbnb had almost no revenue, no real traction, and founders who were paying for the company on a stack of credit cards. Around that time, Paul Graham asked Brian Chesky whether people were actually doing this — renting out rooms in their own homes to strangers over the internet. Chesky said yes. Graham asked what was wrong with them. It wasn't a promising interview.

That same fall, during the Democratic and Republican National Conventions, the founders needed money and came up with something almost nobody would have predicted from a "travel" company: breakfast cereal. Obama-O's — "the breakfast of change" — and Cap'n McCain's — "a maverick in every bite." General Mills wouldn't return their calls. A local printer made them a thousand boxes. The story hit national news within a week, and the founders sold $40,000 worth of cereal — more than the $5,000 the actual Airbnb website had made that entire year.

Chesky doesn't tell this story because it's funny, though it is. He tells it because it's the origin of one of Airbnb's actual, permanent core values: be a cereal entrepreneur — meaning, be resourceful and creative under real constraint, because constraint is what forces creativity in the first place. That's the subject of this chapter: not culture as a poster on a wall, but culture as the set of values a company's founding moments actually produce, deliberately written down, and then protected for as long as the company exists.

💡 The Core Idea: A Fill-in-the-Blank Definition, and Why It's Not Optional

Alfred Lin, an early investor in Zappos and Airbnb, offers a useful skeleton for a definition that otherwise tends to stay vague: company culture is the core values and the actions of every team member, every day, in pursuit of the company's mission. Fill in those three blanks honestly for any company, and you have a working definition of its actual culture — not the one printed in the employee handbook, the one people actually operate by.

Why this matters enough to spend a whole chapter on: culture is what a team falls back on to make decisions when there's no time to ask the founder directly. It's what tells people what not to do — often the more valuable half of the instruction. It's what lets you tell, cleanly, who should stay and who shouldn't, when the honest answer would otherwise be complicated and political. And it's not just a soft, feel-good asset: looking at the Fortune 100 Best Companies to Work For against the S&P 500 and the Russell 3000 from 1994 to 2013, the "best places to work" index returned roughly 11.8% — close to double the other two. Trust and strong culture, in other words, show up in stock returns, not just in employee satisfaction surveys.

Patrick Collison, teaching a related lecture in the same course, offers the sharpest explanation of why culture becomes so important as a company grows, rather than staying a nice-to-have: culture is a solution to a bandwidth problem. In an ideally small company, a founder could be involved in every decision. That stops being physically possible almost immediately — by somewhere around 150 people, in Collison's estimate, it's completely hopeless. Culture is the thing you deliberately design to stay constant as the fraction of decisions you can personally touch keeps shrinking toward zero. Get it right early, and the company keeps making decisions the way you would have, long after you've stopped being in the room.

🔍 Why It's True: How Real Companies Actually Wrote Theirs Down

Zappos: a year, thirty-seven values, and a whittling process

When Zappos went through the exercise of defining its own values, employees proposed thirty-seven candidates. The company spent roughly a year narrowing that list down to about ten. That length of time is itself a lesson: a value like "honesty" sounds appealingly simple, but it's nearly meaningless as written, because no company would ever claim the opposite. Nobody sets out to build a culture of dishonesty. A real value has to say something that could plausibly not be true of some other company — which is why it takes real time and real specificity to get right.

Zappos landed on specifics like "deliver wow through service" — not just "great customer service," but a claim about the degree and quality of service, tied directly to the company's actual mission. Alongside it: "be humble," which came from the opposite direction Alfred Lin recommends founders use when defining values — think about the people you've disliked working with in the past, and write down the opposite of what made them difficult. Zappos's founders didn't like working with arrogant people, so humility became one of their core values, credible precisely because it was a specific reaction to something real.

One more Zappos habit worth naming directly: prioritizing company, then department, then team, then self, when smart people disagree. The point isn't hierarchy for its own sake — it's steering disagreement toward "how do we make this idea better together" rather than toward "who's right," which matters because a room full of talented people arguing to win is a very different room from a room full of talented people building on each other's ideas.

That last point connects to a specific, checkable model of what actually breaks inside teams, borrowed from Patrick Lencioni's The Five Dysfunctions of a Team. The dysfunctions stack on top of each other, each one causing the next:

  1. No trust among team members leads to —
  2. No real conflict or debate. Without trust, people avoid disagreement rather than working through it, which means —
  3. No genuine commitment. If ideas were never actually debated, nobody is sure the final decision is right, so nobody fully commits to it, which means —
  4. No accountability. People who never truly committed don't hold each other, or themselves, to the outcome, which means —
  5. No results.

The practical use of this stack: if a team isn't producing results, the fix usually isn't "try harder" or "communicate more." It's diagnosing which layer actually broke first, since accountability and results are downstream problems that can't be fixed directly — they're symptoms of a trust or conflict problem further up the stack.

Airbnb: writing the values before hiring anyone at all

Zappos wrote its values down at around a hundred employees — successful, but later than its own founders wished in hindsight. Airbnb's founders, after visiting Zappos and learning this, decided to do it earlier: before hiring a single person. As far as Sam Altman has been able to tell, Airbnb may be the only company in Y Combinator's history to have written its core values down before making any hire at all.

Chesky's framing for why this mattered so much: your first engineering hire isn't really one hire. It's closer to a DNA sample for the entire company, because if the company succeeds, there will eventually be a hundred thousand more people like that first one. The real question isn't "can this person do the job." It's "do I want to build an organization out of a hundred thousand people who think and behave the way this person does." Airbnb's first engineer took four to five months to hire — a number that echoes Chapter 5's account of Airbnb's five-month search for its very first employee overall, because it's describing the same hire.

Chesky distinguishes two layers inside "culture," and the distinction matters: there are behaviors — the specific rituals and practices a company follows — and there are core values underneath them. Behaviors can and should evolve over decades. Core values are supposed to be the three to six things that never change, the things that would still describe the company fifty years from now even if almost everything else about how it operates looks completely different. Steve Jobs's own version of this distinction, quoted by Chesky: "Our products change but our values never have." Chesky's broader observation is that this isn't unique to companies — nations do the same thing with founding declarations, precisely so they can endure past any single leader.

Missionaries versus mercenaries

The clearest test of whether a company's stated values are real or decorative is what happens when following them costs something. Airbnb's version of this test arrived in 2011: a company founded by the Samwer brothers, known for aggressively cloning successful American startups and building them out at extreme speed, copied Airbnb across Europe. Within thirty days, the clone had hired four hundred people. The pragmatic case for acquiring them was strong — Airbnb, still only forty employees and seven million dollars raised at the time, had no real path to matching that speed on its own, and a travel company that isn't in Europe is missing a critical piece of the map.

Chesky chose not to buy them. His own explanation: "I felt like we were missionaries and they were mercenaries," and he believed — as a bet, not a certainty — that missionaries outlast mercenaries in the long run, and that the best response to being cloned was simply to keep building the real thing better than the clone ever could. This was, by his own account, a genuinely controversial call inside the company at the time. It's worth being honest that it wasn't an obviously correct decision in the moment — reasonable people on Airbnb's own team argued for buying the clone. What makes it a useful example for this chapter isn't that it was risk-free. It's that Airbnb had actually written down, in advance, what kind of company it wanted to be — which meant that when a real, costly decision arrived, there was already a values-based answer to reach for instead of improvising one under pressure. More than half of Airbnb's revenue eventually came from Europe anyway.

Culture and brand are the same coin, seen from two sides

A recurring theme across the founders in this chapter: culture (what you believe internally) and brand (what customers believe about you) aren't separate projects. Zappos's own framing is that they're two sides of one coin — whatever a company actually believes, internally, eventually leaks out into how customers experience it, whether or not that was intended. Google's internal hiring shorthand — asking whether a candidate "is Google" — captures the same idea from the inside. There's no such thing, in this framing, as a good culture or a bad culture in the abstract. There's only a strong culture or a weak one, and a strong culture that fits one company might be entirely wrong for another.

Airbnb learned this the hard way with its own external messaging. Its early tagline — "forget hotels, save money with Airbnb" — described the company as a cheaper substitute for something else, which is a fundamentally weak position: a company selling on price alone is, in Chesky's words, "a utility," and utilities get commoditized and priced down over time. The company's later language — "travel like a human," built around belonging rather than savings — matched what Airbnb's founders actually believed the company was for. Steve Jobs made the identical pivot at Apple with the 1997 "Think Different" campaign: not a word about processor speed or specifications, entirely about what the company believed. Chesky's summary of a founder's actual communications job: you articulate the vision, then keep repeating it — in recruiting conversations, investor meetings, press interviews, customer emails — until it's said itself so many times that it's simply what people believe about the company, largely because it's true.

Culture has to extend past your own employees, to the people your product depends on

Chesky is candid that Airbnb got this one wrong for years before correcting it. In the company's earliest days, he took what he calls "the Craig Newmark school of thought" — named for Craigslist's founder — that anyone should be able to use Airbnb: if you wanted to rent out your place, you simply could, with no cultural gate at all. Most hosts, it turned out, genuinely believed in the same values the company did. But a meaningful minority didn't — they'd found a way to make money renting out a home, full stop, with no particular attachment to what Airbnb was actually trying to build — and Chesky is direct that this group caused a disproportionate share of the company's real problems. The lesson landed late: hosts aren't just users of the product, they're partners in it, and partners need to actually believe in the same culture employees are hired against, not merely comply with a terms-of-service agreement. Airbnb's fix was the Superhost program — a status hosts have to actively earn by demonstrating the company's values in practice, in exchange for priority support and better placement in search results. The broader point generalizes past Airbnb specifically: a culture that only governs the people on your payroll, while leaving the people your product actually depends on entirely ungoverned, has simply moved its weakest link outside the building instead of fixing it.

Transparency scales, but not automatically

Culture at ten people and culture at two hundred people require different tools, even if the underlying values stay fixed. Stripe's founders made an unusually aggressive bet on one specific value — internal transparency — as the company grew, and their experience shows both the upside and the real cost of taking a value that seriously. Early on, nearly every email at Stripe went to every employee by default, on the theory that shared context eliminates a large share of the meetings and misunderstandings that otherwise accumulate as a company grows. Ben Silbermann of Pinterest, comparing notes with Stripe's founders, describes the underlying design philosophy well: culture is more like gardening than architecture. You plant values early, and then you spend ongoing effort pulling weeds and helping the right things grow — it's not a structure you build once and leave alone.

The costs of Stripe's transparency bet were real, not hypothetical. At roughly fifty employees, new hires reported being overwhelmed rather than informed — too much email to find what actually mattered to them. At one point, internal email volume grew large enough to break Gmail itself. The fix wasn't abandoning the value; it was building purpose-made tooling (mailing-list structures, a company-wide shared-documents system) and establishing social norms about what should and shouldn't be shared as openly, once open sharing itself was happening in front of a much larger and more anonymous audience than the first ten people in one room. The lesson generalizes past transparency specifically: a value that worked effortlessly at ten people usually needs real, deliberate infrastructure to keep working at two hundred — it doesn't scale for free just because it was true from day one.

⚠️ Common Mistakes

  • Treating culture as a one-time document instead of a daily habit. Alfred Lin's comparison: culture is like fitness. You can't do a single hard session and then coast — skip it for a while, and it deteriorates the same way physical fitness does.
  • Writing values so generic that no company would ever claim the opposite. "Honesty" and "integrity" sound safe, but they don't tell anyone how to actually behave differently from a competitor. A real value should be specific enough to be falsifiable.
  • Interviewing only for skill, not for whether someone actually believes in the mission. A brilliant hire who doesn't believe in what the company is doing tends not to put in the sustained effort the mission actually requires.
  • Assuming a value that worked at ten people will keep working at two hundred without any new investment. Stripe's own transparency bet needed real tooling and new norms once it stopped being ten people in one room.
  • Only discovering what the company's values mean under pressure, instead of before it. Airbnb had already decided, before the Samwer brothers ever appeared, that it wanted to be a company of "missionaries" — which is what made a genuinely hard decision resolvable instead of just another argument.

⚖️ Tradeoffs and Edge Cases

Was the missionaries-versus-mercenaries decision actually correct? Worth being honest here: it's not possible to prove, even in hindsight, that acquiring the Samwer brothers' clone would have gone worse for Airbnb. The pragmatic case for buying speed in a new, unfamiliar continent was real, and reasonable people inside the company argued for it. What this chapter is actually claiming is narrower and more defensible: having a written, real set of values in advance turned an agonizing, ambiguous decision into one Airbnb's founders could reason through quickly and stand behind, rather than making it up under pressure with no anchor at all. A company without that anchor doesn't necessarily make the wrong call — it just has no faster way to make the right one.

Isn't a company this focused on mission and values really just a marketing exercise? This is a real question people asked Chesky directly, and it's worth taking seriously rather than dismissing. Sequoia's Doug Leone once told Chesky, half-joking, that his job was the worst in his entire portfolio: Airbnb is a technology company facing every challenge a normal software startup faces, plus operating in effectively every country in the world, plus being a payments company handling billions of dollars with real fraud and regulatory exposure, plus a trust-and-safety operation on the scale of governing a mid-size city (Leone's comparison: imagine being responsible, every single night, for everything that happens among the roughly 425,000 people staying in strangers' homes on a typical peak night, across 190 countries), plus navigating distinct regulations in thirty thousand different cities, plus a genuinely hard search-and-matching problem, since there's no single "best" listing in a city the way there might be a single best search result for a query. None of that is marketing. The point of this chapter isn't that culture replaces solving those problems — it's that a real, specific culture is what makes an organization capable of solving all of them at once, coherently, without a founder personally supervising every decision.

Does writing values down early guarantee anything? No — Zappos wrote its values at a hundred employees and still built one of the most-cited culture examples in business. Earlier is better, mainly because it means fewer bad habits have already calcified by the time you do it. But "we haven't done this yet and we already have fifty employees" is a reason to do it now, not a reason to conclude it's too late to matter.

🧪 Applying the Test

Try Alfred Lin's actual exercise, the one Zappos used to generate its own list. First, write down the people you've most enjoyed working with, and name specifically what made them good to work with — not vague traits, but things you could point to. Then write down people you've actively disliked working with, and name the opposite. Somewhere in that contrast is the beginning of a real values list — not one copied from another company's careers page, but one built from your own specific, lived experience of what good and bad collaboration actually look like.

Then apply Chesky's harder test to it: if you had to describe your company in three to six words that would still be true in fifty years, regardless of what the product looks like by then, what would they be? If the honest answer is closer to "we're not sure yet," that's useful information — it means the exercise above is worth doing now, before the company is large enough to make doing it much harder.

✅ Chapter Summary

  • Culture is the core values and daily actions of every team member, in pursuit of the company's mission — not a poster, a real, checkable pattern of behavior.
  • Culture matters because it's how decisions get made when a founder can't be involved in every one — Patrick Collison's framing: culture is a solution to a bandwidth problem that gets worse as the company grows.
  • The best-places-to-work index outperformed the S&P 500 and Russell 3000 by nearly 2x from 1994–2013 — strong culture shows up in financial returns, not just morale.
  • Write values from lived experience — who you loved and hated working with — not from generic, unfalsifiable words like "honesty" that no company would ever claim the opposite of.
  • Separate behaviors (which can evolve for decades) from core values (which shouldn't change) — Airbnb wrote its values before hiring anyone; Zappos wrote its own at a hundred employees and still succeeded.
  • The real test of a stated value is what it costs you to follow it under pressure — Airbnb's choice not to acquire a well-funded clone is the clearest example, and it's worth being honest that it wasn't a risk-free, obviously correct call.
