Schultz's answer is that you can usually figure this out yourself with publicly available numbers, using the same kind of rough, order-of-magnitude reasoning that let physicists estimate the yield of a nuclear test from a single published photograph, using nothing but the visible dimensions of the blast and some basic physics. You don't need precision. You need a ballpark.

Applied to a real product: if there are roughly 2 billion people online worldwide, and Facebook has around 1.3 billion active users, that ratio alone tells you Facebook has captured a very large share of everyone who could plausibly use it — a signal, on its own, that its retention must be extraordinarily strong. The same trick works for WhatsApp, which had around 600 million active users, against the global smartphone population, or Amazon against the population of its home market. None of these give you an exact figure. All of them tell you whether you're in the right neighborhood.

The right neighborhood, importantly, is different for every category. Retaining 20–30% of users on a monthly basis is a genuinely strong result for an e-commerce business. That same number would be a catastrophic result for a social network, where the earliest cohort of users typically needs to stay around 80% retained for the product to have any real chance of becoming huge. Benchmarking your own retention against the wrong category's expectations — comparing a social product's numbers to an e-commerce company's, or the reverse — will make a genuinely fine result look like a failure, or a genuinely bad one look acceptable.

The North Star metric, and why it matters more once you're not in the room

Once retention shows a real, flattening curve, the next problem is coordinating an entire company's day-to-day decisions around growing it. Schultz's advice here is deliberately contrarian: a startup shouldn't build a separate "growth team" walled off from the rest of the company. The whole company should function as the growth team, and the CEO — not a specialized hire — should personally be the one setting the target everyone else optimizes toward.

That target is what Schultz calls a North Star metric: a single number, chosen deliberately, that the entire company holds itself accountable to. Facebook's version was monthly active users, not total registrations — a specific decision by Mark Zuckerberg to measure and publish the number that actually reflected engagement, rather than the larger, more flattering number of everyone who'd ever signed up. WhatsApp measured messages sent. Airbnb measured nights booked. eBay measured Gross Merchandise Volume — the total value of goods moving through the platform — rather than eBay's own revenue, since GMV was the number that actually captured how much commerce the platform was enabling: eBay's GMV consistently ran at roughly 10 times its reported revenue, so a metric anchored to revenue alone would have badly understated the business. eBay's own leaders — Pierre Omidyar, Meg Whitman, and John Donahoe, across their respective tenures — consistently pointed back to that GMV figure rather than letting revenue stand in for it.

The reason a single, clearly chosen metric matters this much connects back to an idea this book already covered from a completely different angle: Patrick Collison's observation, in Chapter 6, that culture is a solution to a bandwidth problem — the thing that keeps decisions aligned once a founder can't personally be in every room. A North Star metric does the identical job for growth decisions specifically. You cannot control what a hundred different employees each decide to prioritize on a given afternoon. What you can do is make sure they're all optimizing toward the same specific number, so that even a decision made without you in the room tends to push in the right direction.

eBay's own affiliate program is a clean, concrete illustration of how much the choice of metric shapes behavior. The company originally paid affiliates for every confirmed registered user (CRU) they brought in — someone who signed up and verified their account. Switching the payment model to reward activated confirmed registered users (ACRUs) — people who went on to actually bid on or list an item — caused total CRUs to drop by about 20% overnight. But ACRUs, the number that actually mattered, dropped by only about 5%, and then began growing faster than before. The mechanism: paying for raw registrations sent affiliates traffic straight to a generic sign-up page, with no compelling reason to complete it. Paying for activation sent that same traffic to an actual search results page — showing someone searching for a specific item exactly what they wanted — which is what Schultz calls the magic moment: the specific point where a new user genuinely understands, viscerally, what your product is for.

Every product has its own magic moment, and identifying it precisely is worth real effort. Facebook's is seeing a friend's face in your feed for the first time — which is why the company measured and optimized hard for getting new users to ten friends within their first fourteen days, treating that specific number as a leading indicator of whether the magic moment had landed. Airbnb's is finding the one listing you actually want to stay in. eBay's is finding the exact collectible you've been looking for. None of these are abstract — they're the specific instant a new user stops evaluating your product and starts using it because they want to.

Optimize for the user who's about to leave, not the user you already have

A subtle, easy-to-miss mistake Schultz calls out directly: when a company thinks about its own product, it naturally thinks from the perspective of its most engaged users — the people already using it constantly, the people most like the founders themselves. Growth decisions made from that perspective are frequently wrong, because the most engaged users are not the ones whose behavior determines whether the company keeps growing.

His example is notifications. Power users complain about getting too many. The founder's instinct, hearing that complaint, is to reduce notification volume across the board. But the growth-relevant question isn't "are my best users annoyed" — it's "is the person who's about to churn getting the one notification that would have brought them back." Schultz's own growth-accounting framework at Facebook broke users into three buckets: new users, resurrected users (people who'd been gone for 30+ days and came back), and churned users. Once a product has been around for a few years, resurrected and churned users dominate the numbers far more than new signups do — and the users in both of those categories reliably had low friend counts and had never really been connected to the product's magic moment in the first place. The fix wasn't reducing notifications for everyone. It was increasing the specific notifications that helped low-connection users reach the magic moment, while leaving already-engaged power users alone.

⚠️ Common Mistakes

  • Investing in growth tactics before the retention curve has actually flattened. Virality, paid acquisition, and dedicated growth hires all amplify a product that already works. None of them fix a product that doesn't.
  • Building a separate "growth team" instead of making growth the whole company's job, led personally by the CEO.
  • Benchmarking retention against the wrong category. A perfectly healthy e-commerce retention rate looks catastrophic by social-network standards, and the reverse is just as misleading.
  • Choosing a North Star metric that flatters the company rather than one that reflects real engagement — total registrations instead of monthly active users, revenue instead of the volume of value actually flowing through the platform.
  • Designing growth decisions around your own experience, or your most engaged users', instead of the marginal user who is about to leave.

⚖️ Tradeoffs and Edge Cases

What if the retention curve looks ambiguous — not clearly flattening, but not clearly failing either? This is a real, common case, and Chapter 7 already covered the practical answer from a different angle: Cheung's advice was to set a specific, honest growth plan in advance and treat three or four consecutive weeks of no improvement as the signal to reconsider, rather than waiting for total clarity that may never arrive. The retention curve and the growth-plan check are two views of the same underlying question, and it's worth using both together rather than either alone.

Does every company need exactly one North Star metric, forever? Not necessarily forever — a company's North Star can and sometimes should evolve as the business matures. What matters more than permanence is clarity at any given time: everyone in the company, at any moment, should be able to name the same single metric if asked, the way eBay's leadership consistently pointed to Gross Merchandise Volume rather than letting different teams quietly optimize for revenue, registrations, or listings instead.

Isn't optimizing for the "marginal user" in tension with building a great product for your best users? Not in practice, though it can feel that way. Chapter 8 already established that a great product requires deep, direct engagement with real users — that's still true, and still necessary for product quality. The distinction Schultz is making is narrower: product quality decisions can reasonably lean on your most engaged users' feedback, but growth decisions specifically — the ones about who to notify, re-engage, or push toward the magic moment — need to be evaluated from the perspective of users who haven't gotten there yet, not the ones who already have.

🧪 Applying the Test

Using only public information, make a rough estimate of your own product's plausible retention ceiling the way Schultz estimated Facebook's: what's the total realistic population who could ever use what you're building, and how does your current active user count compare to it? Then identify your product's magic moment as specifically as you can — the exact instant a new user would say "oh, that's what this is for" — and check honestly whether your onboarding gets a new user there quickly, or buries it behind several steps that have nothing to do with it.

✅ Chapter Summary

  • Retention is the actual foundation of growth — a great product produces customers, and whether those customers stay is what determines whether the company actually grows.
  • A retention curve that flattens (asymptotic) signals real product-market fit. A curve that keeps declining toward zero signals its absence — Cheung's cohort-analysis method from Chapter 7 and Schultz's growth-forecasting method arrive at the identical tool independently.
  • If your retention curve hasn't flattened, don't invest in growth tactics yet — fix the product first.
  • What counts as "good" retention differs by category — 20–30% monthly is strong for e-commerce; a social product typically needs closer to 80% in its earliest cohort.
  • A single, clearly chosen North Star metric — not a separate growth team — keeps a whole company's decisions aligned even when the founder isn't in the room, the same role culture plays in Chapter 6.
  • What you choose to measure and pay for shapes real behavior — eBay's shift from paying for registrations to paying for activated users changed what its affiliates actually did.
  • Every product has a specific "magic moment" worth identifying precisely and driving new users toward as fast as possible.
  • Growth decisions should be optimized for the marginal user who's about to leave, not for your own experience or your most engaged users.

📝 Exercises

  1. Recall: Explain why a retention curve that keeps sloping toward zero is a signal to stop investing in growth tactics rather than a signal to invest harder in them.
  2. Apply: Identify your own product's (or a product idea's) magic moment as specifically as you can. What does your current onboarding flow actually do to get a new user there quickly, and what's standing in the way?
  3. Apply: Choose a North Star metric for a real or hypothetical product, and explain why it's a better choice than the two or three other metrics you considered and rejected.
  4. Confront a counterexample: This chapter argues against having a dedicated growth team. But many well-known, successful companies do have dedicated growth teams and growth hires. Under what conditions does a dedicated growth team make sense, and how would you tell whether your company has actually earned the right to have one yet?

📚 Further Reading

  • Alex Schultz's original CS183B lecture covers additional growth-accounting detail (the new/resurrected/churned framework) beyond what this chapter had room for.
  • Chapter 11 picks up directly where this chapter leaves off, covering the specific channels and tactics (virality, SEO, paid acquisition) that only work once retention is already solid.
  • Chapter 6's discussion of culture as "a solution to a bandwidth problem" is worth rereading alongside this chapter's North Star metric section — they're the same underlying mechanism applied to two different problems.
🌱

Chapter 11 — Growth Channels and Tactics

When Hotmail launched, several well-funded competitors were already running television ads and billboard campaigns for free webmail. Hotmail had far less money. What it had instead was a single line of text, appended automatically to the bottom of every email its users sent: "Sent from Hotmail. Get your free email here." No production budget, no media buy — just a sentence that rode along on every message a user was already sending anyway. Hotmail outgrew its better-funded rivals.

That single footer line is a clean example of a channel doing exactly what a channel is supposed to do: amplify something that already works. Chapter 10 established that retention has to come first. This chapter picks up from there — assuming a retention curve that actually flattens — and covers the specific channels and tactics that turn a working product into a growing one: virality, search, messaging, international expansion, and paid acquisition.

💡 The Core Idea: Three Kinds of Growth, One Question Underneath All of Them

Adora Cheung, building Homejoy, organizes growth into three categories: sticky growth (getting existing customers to come back and spend more), viral growth (existing customers bringing in new ones), and paid growth (spending money directly to acquire customers). Every tactic in this chapter is a specific implementation of one of these three.

The question that determines whether any of them is worth pursuing is the same one underneath all three: is this sustainable? A viral loop with no retention behind it produces a spike that evaporates. Paid acquisition that costs more than a customer is ever worth produces a business that loses money on every sale, no matter how much revenue it books. Sticky growth is the safest of the three by construction, since it's just retention (already covered in Chapter 10) compounding on itself — but it's also the slowest, which is exactly why founders reach for the other two.

🔍 Why It's True: The Mechanics of Each Channel

Virality has a formula, and it explains why different products go viral differently

Sean Parker's framework, adopted at Facebook, breaks virality into three multiplied factors: payload (how many people a single "viral blast" can reach), conversion rate (what fraction of those people actually sign up), and frequency (how often the blast happens). Multiply enough of a user base through those three factors and you get a K-factor — roughly, how many new users each existing user brings in. A K-factor below 1 means the mechanism alone will eventually flatline. Above 1, and it compounds.

The formula also explains why superficially different products can each be "viral" in completely different ways. Hotmail had a low payload (one email at a time) but very high frequency (people email constantly) and very high conversion (anyone still tied to a slow, ISP-locked email address had a real, immediate reason to switch). PayPal had low payload and low frequency, but an extremely high conversion rate on both sides of its two-sided market: telling a seller "I'm going to send you money this way" converts almost automatically, and offering a new user real cash ($10 for signing up, $10 for the friend who referred them) converts nearly as well. Facebook, notably, wasn't viral in either of these senses — it had no built-in mechanism for emailing someone outside the network the way Hotmail or PayPal did. It grew through old-fashioned word of mouth, driven by a product good enough that people talked about it anyway, which is a reminder that virality is one path to growth, not the only one, and the retention work from Chapter 10 is what made the word-of-mouth version possible at all.

The K-factor calculation itself compounds a chain of conversion rates — a framework credited to Ed, who ran growth at Uber after doing the same job at Facebook, extending Parker's payload/conversion/frequency model into a concrete funnel: of everyone who imports their contacts, how many send invites; of those invited, how many click; of those who click, how many sign up; of those who sign up, how many go on to import their own contacts and repeat the cycle. Worked through with real numbers: if 100 people get an invite per person who imports their contacts, and 10% of those invited click, and 50% of those who click sign up, and only 10–20% of new signups go on to import their own contacts, the resulting K-factor lands somewhere around 0.5–1.0 — not quite viral on its own.

Figure 11.1 — The invite-chain loop Ed's framework compounds into a K-factor

  imports contacts --> sends invites --> invite is clicked --> recipient signs up
        ^   (payload: e.g. 100                (conversion:            |
        |    people per importer)              10% click,             |
        |                                       then 50% of            |
        |                                       clicks sign up)        |
        +---------- some of these new signups import their own --------+
                     contacts too (only 10-20%) and repeat the loop

              K-factor = payload x conversion (click) x conversion (signup)
                          x share of signups who repeat the loop

A cautionary case worth naming directly: some products, like the short-video app Viddy, pushed their K-factor comfortably above 1 through aggressive prompts and still collapsed, because none of that viral growth was backed by real retention underneath it. Virality without retention doesn't just under-deliver — it can actively mislead a team into believing they've solved a problem they haven't touched.

Search: the wrong keyword, and the power of one navigation change

Search engine optimization breaks into three parts worth separating clearly. First, keyword research — and getting this wrong is a specific, avoidable failure mode. Schultz describes spending a full year optimizing an early cocktail website to rank for "cocktail making," dominating that exact term completely — only to discover that, in the UK market he was targeting, "cocktail making" was searched about 500 times a month. Ranking first for it delivered roughly 400 visitors a month. The much larger search volume was for "cocktail recipes" (or "drink recipes" in the U.S.) — a completely different phrase describing functionally the same intent. Winning the wrong keyword perfectly is still losing, and the gap between those two numbers is the whole lesson: a year of effort had bought a rounding error of traffic.

Second, links and authority — the mechanism underneath most of what Google's ranking algorithm rewards. The clearest illustration of how much this can matter: early in Facebook's public-profile push, the company had built public profile pages, but they were buried behind several clicks with no direct path in from anywhere on the site. Google's crawlers, unable to find an efficient way to those pages, effectively ignored them. Adding a single directory page that linked to public profiles more directly — one structural change — multiplied SEO traffic to those pages roughly a hundredfold. Third, basic technical hygiene (sitemaps, correct headers) matters, but it's the least differentiating of the three; most of the real leverage sits in doing the first two well.

Messaging channels reward relevance, not volume

Email, SMS, and push notifications share a set of rules, starting with deliverability — a channel you can't reliably reach an inbox through isn't a growth channel at all, whatever its theoretical potential. Beyond that baseline, the rule that matters most is relevance: a blanket newsletter sent identically to every user, regardless of how long they've used the product or how engaged they are, treats a brand-new user and a three-year veteran as though they need the same message, which they almost never do.

The better default is triggered, contextual messaging tied to a specific, personal event. A brand-new user's first "like" or first connection is a magic moment worth celebrating with a notification. The same event, happening for the thousandth time to a veteran user with hundreds of connections, is noise, and notifying them about it risks training them to ignore or disable notifications altogether — which then makes it harder to reach them with a message that genuinely does matter later. eBay's own best-performing email campaign, by click-through rate, was triggered by a customer's very first cross-border transaction — a specific, timely, personally relevant event, not a generic blast sent to the whole user base on a schedule.

Growing into other languages and markets is a long-lead investment, not a feature flag

Facebook internationalized later than it should have, by its own leadership's admission, and paid a real cost for the delay: while the product stayed U.S.-focused, clones sprang up across the world — some blatant enough to leave the site's own template filenames unchanged in the copied code. Catching up required building real infrastructure, not just translating text: an internal system for extracting every string in the product, plus a community translation platform that let Facebook's own users translate the product themselves rather than waiting on professional translators alone. French was fully translated within twelve hours of that system going live. Within a few years, the product supported over a hundred languages, the large majority contributed by the community rather than paid translators.

The subtler lesson sits in which languages got prioritized first, and it's a direct echo of Chapter 2's "why now" argument applied to markets instead of products: build for where the world is going, not where it already is. The four languages that mattered most in Facebook's early international push — French, Italian, German, Spanish — mostly shrank in relative importance within a few years, while languages that barely registered at first, like Hindi, quadrupled. A translation and localization strategy built only around today's largest markets would have missed the shift entirely; the infrastructure that mattered was the kind flexible enough to expand into whichever language turned out to matter next.

Paid acquisition: a worked example

Paid growth is the most directly quantifiable of the three categories, which is exactly why it deserves real numbers rather than just a description. Two formulas govern whether paid acquisition is a sound investment or a leaky bucket:

CAC (customer acquisition cost) = cost per click ÷ conversion rate

Profit per customer = CLV (customer lifetime value) − CAC

Here is an illustrative example to make the formula concrete — the dollar figures below are invented for demonstration, not numbers Cheung gave in her lecture, though the two formulas themselves are hers. Suppose an ad costs $2 per click, and 4% of the people who click go on to sign up. CAC is $2 ÷ 0.04 = $50 per acquired customer. If that customer is worth $300 to the business over their first year (their CLV), the math looks straightforwardly good: $300 − $50 = $250 in profit per customer acquired. Multiply that across enough customers and paid acquisition looks like free money.

Two complications make this less simple in practice, and both are worth taking seriously before scaling a paid channel. First, CAC and CLV both vary enormously by customer segment, and averaging across all customers hides that variation in a dangerous way. A marketplace for country music memorabilia, to use an intentionally vivid example, will see a far higher CLV from a customer in Nashville than from one in a market with almost no interest in the category — buying ads at a single blended CAC, rather than a CAC set per segment, means overpaying badly in the low-value segments while potentially underpaying (and under-investing) in the high-value ones.

Second, the timing of when a customer actually pays you back matters as much as whether they eventually do. Say a customer is genuinely worth $300 over twelve months, but only $100 of that arrives in the first month, with the rest spread across the rest of the year. If the CAC to acquire that customer is $200, the business is $100 underwater on that customer until the remaining $200 of value actually arrives — and if something goes wrong before then (the customer churns early, the market shifts, the company runs low on cash), that gap never closes. A payback period of around three months is a conservative, safe default; stretching to twelve months is riskier but sometimes reasonable for a well-capitalized company; going beyond twelve months moves into territory where a founder is effectively betting the company's cash position on a prediction about a customer's future behavior that hasn't been tested yet.

⚠️ Common Mistakes

  • Chasing a K-factor above 1 without retention underneath it. Viddy's own story is the direct cautionary example: real virality, no durable product, ultimately no company.
  • Winning the wrong keyword perfectly. A full year spent dominating "cocktail making" produced a fraction of the traffic "cocktail recipes" would have, because nobody was searching for the first phrase.
  • Sending the same newsletter to every user regardless of how engaged they already are. A first-time magic moment and a thousandth repetition of the same event call for completely different messages, if they call for a message at all.
  • Calculating CAC and CLV as a single blended average across all customers, hiding enormous segment-level variation that should be driving very different acquisition spend in different segments.
  • Accepting a long payback period without the cash reserves to survive it if a customer's expected value doesn't materialize on schedule.
  • Waiting too long to invest in international infrastructure, and then discovering competitors have already cloned the product into markets you weren't paying attention to.

⚖️ Tradeoffs and Edge Cases

Doesn't the whole "virality formula" framing suggest every product needs a formal referral loop? No — Facebook's own history is the clearest evidence against that assumption. It grew primarily through word of mouth, without ever building a Hotmail- or PayPal-style formal viral mechanic, because the product itself was compelling enough to talk about. The formula in this chapter is a tool for understanding and improving a viral mechanic where one exists and makes sense for the product, not a requirement that every company build one.

How aggressive should paid acquisition be, given the payback-period risk described above? This depends heavily on how much of a cash cushion a company actually has, which is a question this book returns to properly in Part Five. The short version for now: treat the three-month payback benchmark as the safe default, and only extend it further if you have both the capital reserves to survive the wait and real confidence — not hope — that the later-arriving value will actually show up on schedule.

Is international expansion always worth the infrastructure investment this chapter describes? Not for every company at every stage — it's a genuinely large, long-lead investment, and building it too early, before a company has solid domestic product-market fit, can be a serious distraction. The lesson from Facebook's own experience isn't "internationalize immediately." It's "don't wait until competitors have already claimed the markets you'll eventually want, and when you do build for other languages, build for where those markets are headed rather than only their current size."

🧪 Applying the Test

Pick one growth channel you're considering (or already using) and run the actual math from this chapter's worked example on it: what's your real CAC, broken out by at least two meaningfully different customer segments rather than blended into one average? What would your CLV need to be, in each segment, for that channel to be worth scaling? If you don't currently know your CLV by segment, that's the more urgent problem to solve before spending more on the channel itself.

✅ Chapter Summary

  • Growth channels only amplify a product that already retains users — none of the tactics in this chapter substitute for the product-market fit covered in Chapter 10.
  • Virality has a real formula: payload × conversion rate × frequency determines your K-factor, and different products (Hotmail, PayPal, Facebook) go viral in structurally different ways.
  • Virality without retention is actively misleading — Viddy pushed its K-factor above 1 and still collapsed, because nothing durable sat underneath the growth.
  • SEO success depends on researching the right keyword (not just winning whichever one you chose) and on links and site structure — a single navigation change multiplied Facebook's profile-page SEO traffic roughly a hundredfold.
  • Messaging channels reward relevance over volume — a triggered, personally relevant message beats a generic blast sent to everyone at once.
  • International growth requires long-lead infrastructure investment, prioritized toward where a market is headed, not just where it is today.
  • Paid acquisition math (CAC = cost per click ÷ conversion rate; profit = CLV − CAC) only gives an honest answer when broken out by customer segment, and payback timing matters as much as the final CLV number.

📝 Exercises

  1. Recall: Explain why Facebook is described in this chapter as growing through word of mouth rather than through a formal viral loop, and why that doesn't contradict the virality framework covered earlier in the chapter.
  2. Apply: Using this chapter's CAC/CLV worked example as a template, calculate CAC and expected profit per customer for a real or hypothetical paid channel, broken out by at least two different customer segments.
  3. Apply: Design one triggered, contextual message (email, SMS, or push) for a product you're building, tied to a specific, personally relevant event — not a generic weekly update.
  4. Confront a counterexample: This chapter argues international expansion should be prioritized toward where a market is headed rather than its current size. What risks does that approach carry if the prediction about where a market is headed turns out to be wrong, and how would a founder hedge against that risk?

📚 Further Reading

  • Alex Schultz's original CS183B lecture covers additional detail on Facebook's international growth strategy and its internal translation tooling, plus a "mousetrap" analogy for why a growth mechanism has to actually work before it's worth amplifying, and a reference to Ben Silverman's early growth work at Pinterest.
  • David Ogilvy, Ogilvy on Advertising — recommended in Schultz's lecture as a classic primer on the persuasion side of paid and messaging channels.
  • Adam Penenberg, Viral Loop — recommended in Schultz's lecture for a longer history of viral growth mechanics across several companies.
  • Adora Cheung's lecture (also covered in Chapter 7) is the source of the sticky/viral/paid growth taxonomy this chapter builds on.
  • Chapter 13 returns to unit economics from the fundraising side — investors will ask about CAC, CLV, and payback period using exactly the framework introduced in this chapter's worked example.
  • Appendix C collects every formula from this chapter and Chapter 10 — K-factor, CAC, CLV — on a single page for quick reference.
📰

Chapter 12 — Getting Press

PayPal was in the news for freezing merchant accounts without much explanation, and developers were furious about it. A YC company called WePay saw the opportunity, froze a block of ice with real cash inside it, and dropped it outside a PayPal developer conference — a stunt with essentially no production budget, tied directly to a story already in the news, timed to a specific event where the right reporters would already be standing around with nothing else to write about. It worked. WePay, a company nobody had covered before, got attached to a story everybody was already covering.

That's the pattern this chapter is about: press isn't a meritocracy where the most important company automatically gets covered. It's closer to a specific, learnable skill — knowing what counts as a story, who to tell it to, and how to get a reporter's attention without a budget most startups don't have anyway.

💡 The Core Idea: Goal and Audience Come Before Anything Else

The instinct many first-time founders have is to want press simply because press feels like validation — a sign the company is real and important. Justin Kan's first, blunt correction: if you don't have an actual goal for a piece of coverage, getting it accomplishes nothing for the business, whatever it does for your own feeling of momentum.

The goal determines the audience, and the audience determines which outlets are worth targeting at all. Kan's own examples make the point concrete: Socialcam wanted to be positioned as "video Instagram" for Silicon Valley investors and influencers, so tech press was the target. Exec, a local cleaning service, only needed San Francisco residents who could actually use the service — national coverage would have reached almost entirely the wrong readers, so local outlets like the San Francisco Chronicle were the real target. TwitchTV needed the gaming industry itself — developers and advertisers — to see it as a serious, important place, so the target was gaming industry trade publications and developer blogs, not general consumer press. Three completely different audiences, three completely different outlets, because three completely different business goals sat underneath each campaign.

🔍 Why It's True: What Counts as a Story, and How the Mechanics Actually Work

Not everything you do is a story

The single most useful filter Kan offers: before pitching anything, ask honestly whether you'd want to read this story if you weren't the company's founder. Founders reliably overestimate how interesting their own routine progress is — a minor feature update or a small version bump rarely clears this bar, however meaningful it feels internally.

There are a handful of story types that reliably do clear it: product launches, fundraising announcements (which the press covers readily even though, as Kan notes, they're rarely genuinely interesting), real milestones and metrics, business-strategy stories (usually reserved for companies already established enough to be worth profiling), creative stunts tied to a live news moment (WePay's ice block), hiring announcements for a sufficiently senior or newsworthy hire, and contributor or opinion pieces written by the founder for an outlet.

A story also doesn't need to be original in an absolute sense — it needs to be, in Kan's phrase, "original enough." Being the first company in a specific, narrow category to hit a milestone gets covered; being the second gets ignored, even if the second company is otherwise just as interesting. Kan's own contrast makes the point sharply: you don't want to be the second company to raise $5 million on Kickstarter, because the first one gets all the news and the second gets none of it. The first video game console to raise $10 million on Kickstarter, by contrast, was genuine news, despite many other projects having raised comparable amounts on Kickstarter before it in different categories — the specific combination was new enough to count as a real story.

Treat it like a sales funnel, and get the introduction from someone else's success

Most pitches to most reporters don't convert into coverage, and that's the normal, expected outcome — not a sign the story or the company is bad. The practical mechanics that improve the odds are worth following in order. The single highest-leverage move is getting introduced to a reporter by someone that reporter has already covered successfully, rather than cold-emailing. This benefits everyone involved: the founder making the introduction loses nothing by connecting a reporter to another interesting company, and the reporter, receiving a referral from someone they already decided was worth covering, extends some of that trust to the new introduction by association.

Once in touch, give a reporter real lead time — roughly a week or more before any news needs to run, not a same-day request. Reporters have their own schedules and existing commitments; showing up with no notice reads as a lack of professionalism rather than urgency. Where possible, get an actual phone call or in-person meeting rather than relying only on email exchanges. There's a real, human mechanism behind why this works: the more time and attention a reporter has already invested in a conversation, the more likely they are to follow through and actually write the story, simply because that invested time now feels wasted if they don't.

The pitch itself should be prepared in advance, as a specific set of bullet points memorized well enough to guide the conversation naturally, so the reporter's own notes end up capturing what the founder actually wanted covered — the cofounder's name spelled right, the specific feature or metric that matters, the framing that fits the goal set at the very start. A short follow-up close to the actual publication date, with logistics and any supporting material (photos, video, correct spellings), closes the loop.

PR firms can't tell you what's interesting about your own company

Firms exist to manage contacts and logistics at scale, and that's a real, if limited, value. What they generally cannot do is figure out what's actually newsworthy about a specific company — that judgment has to come from the founder, who understands the business, the product, and the goal well enough to make the call themselves. Kan's own experience with PR firms, across several companies, is that they've never once told him something genuinely interesting about his own company that he hadn't already figured out — only handed him a list of reporters to contact. Combined with real cost (commonly somewhere between five and twenty thousand dollars a month), the case for hiring a firm early is weak. Learning the mechanics in this chapter yourself, first, is both cheaper and more effective — and it's a skill that transfers to every future round of press the company will ever need, in a way that outsourcing the whole function from day one doesn't build.

Press is a vanity metric, and it doesn't scale

It's worth stating plainly, because the temptation to conflate the two is strong: getting covered feels like succeeding, but it isn't the same thing as succeeding. A company can generate a steady stream of press with a flat usage graph and no revenue, and a company can be genuinely thriving with almost no coverage at all. Press correlates loosely with real success because successful companies tend to attract more of it over time — but the correlation runs in that direction, not the reverse, and chasing coverage as a substitute for the metrics covered in Chapters 10 and 11 (retention, growth, revenue) mistakes a symptom for a cause.

It's also worth being explicit about a limit this chapter's advice runs into: press works well as a bootstrapping tool for a company's first hundred, or few hundred, customers — exactly the audience a single well-placed story can plausibly reach. It does not scale into an ongoing acquisition channel the way the tactics in Chapter 11 can, because there is no way to generate an unlimited supply of stories a reporter will find genuinely new. Once a company needs sustained, large-volume growth, this chapter's tools stop being the right tool for that specific job, even though they remain useful for the goals — investor perception, industry credibility, hiring — press is actually good at achieving.

Maintaining relationships pays off later, in both directions

Once a reporter has covered a company, staying in touch — without an immediate ask attached — makes it more likely that reporter covers the company again later, simply because there's now a real relationship rather than a one-time transaction. This matters in both good times and bad: if a company ever faces negative coverage, having an existing relationship with at least a few reporters makes it meaningfully easier to get a fair hearing and get the company's own side of the story included.

The same logic extends outward, not just backward: recommending other founders' stories to reporters, even when there's no direct benefit to you, tends to come back around, because reporters remember who reliably sends them good leads, and other founders remember who helped connect them.

Twitch's own biggest viral moment, "Twitch Plays Pokémon," illustrates how this all fits together honestly. The company didn't invent the idea — a segment of its own community did, entirely organically. Twitch's actual contribution was making sure the groundwork was already in place: enough prior, smaller press coverage that when a major outlet like the BBC went looking for context on this strange new internet phenomenon, there was already a trail of earlier stories to draw on, and a company ready and available to talk to reporters and suggest natural follow-up angles as the story kept developing. Twitch Plays Pokémon itself drew roughly 100,000 people watching at once at its peak, and the relationship-building paid off again on the follow-up: when the community moved on to play through "Crystal," the next Pokémon game, Twitch already had the press contacts in place to turn that into a second round of coverage rather than letting the story die after its first spike. The company didn't manufacture the moment. It had done the unglamorous relationship-building work in advance that let it capitalize on a moment it didn't create.

⚠️ Common Mistakes

  • Pursuing press without a specific goal or target audience. Coverage that reaches the wrong readers doesn't accomplish anything, however good it feels.
  • Pitching a story that isn't actually a story — a minor update that wouldn't interest anyone who isn't already invested in the company's success.
  • Cold-emailing reporters instead of getting a warm introduction from someone they've already covered.
  • Giving a reporter no lead time, then being surprised when a same-day request goes nowhere.
  • Hiring a PR firm early, expecting it to identify what's interesting about the company. That judgment has to come from the founder.
  • Treating press coverage as a proxy for real business success, or as a scalable growth channel once a company needs volume rather than an initial hundred customers.

⚖️ Tradeoffs and Edge Cases

Doesn't calling press "a vanity metric" contradict recommending it as a tool for the first hundred customers? Not really — the two claims describe different uses of the same tool. Press is a legitimate, useful bootstrapping mechanism at very small scale, precisely because a single story genuinely can reach a company's first cohort of users. The warning is against mistaking that early, real usefulness for a signal of the company's overall health, or expecting the same mechanism to keep working at the volume Chapter 11's growth channels are built for.

Is there ever a good time to hire a PR firm? Yes, once a company has a genuine, ongoing cadence of newsworthy events and the volume of contact management becomes a real logistical burden rather than a judgment problem — at that point, a firm's actual strength (managing relationships and logistics at scale) becomes relevant. The mistake this chapter warns against is hiring one early, when the actual bottleneck is deciding what's interesting, not managing contact lists.

What if a company can't identify a "goal" for press beyond general visibility? That's a signal worth taking seriously rather than working around — if there's no specific business objective a piece of coverage would advance, that's a sign this isn't the right time to be pursuing press at all, not a sign to lower the bar for what counts as a goal.

🧪 Applying the Test

Before pitching any story, write down two things separately: the specific business goal this coverage is meant to advance, and the specific audience that goal implies. Then apply the honesty test directly — if you had no connection to this company, would you actually want to read the story you're about to pitch? If the honest answer is no, the fix isn't better writing. It's a different story, or waiting until there's a real one to tell.

✅ Chapter Summary

  • Press is not a meritocracy — define your specific business goal and target audience before pitching anything, since different goals point to completely different outlets.
  • A story has to clear a real bar: would you want to read it if you weren't the founder? Most routine updates don't clear it.
  • A story doesn't need to be original — it needs to be original enough, usually meaning first in some specific, narrow category.
  • Get warm introductions from founders a reporter has already covered, rather than cold emailing; give real lead time; prepare a memorized bullet-point pitch in advance.
  • PR firms manage contacts and logistics well but can't tell you what's actually interesting about your company — that judgment has to be the founder's.
  • Press correlates with looking successful, but it isn't the same as being successful, and it doesn't scale past a company's first few hundred customers the way real growth channels do.
  • Maintain reporter relationships even without an immediate ask, and help other founders get coverage — both tend to come back around later.

📝 Exercises

  1. Recall: Explain why "original enough" is a more useful standard than "completely original" when deciding whether something is a pitchable story.
  2. Apply: Pick a real or hypothetical company. Define its specific press goal and target audience, the way Kan did for Socialcam, Exec, and TwitchTV. What outlets would actually reach that audience?
  3. Apply: Draft the bullet-point pitch you'd bring into a reporter meeting for a real or hypothetical story, structured so a reporter's own notes would capture what you actually wanted covered.
  4. Confront a counterexample: This chapter argues press doesn't scale as a growth channel past a company's first few hundred customers. Are there business models or company types where ongoing press coverage genuinely does function as a durable, scalable growth channel? What would have to be true about that business for it to work?

📚 Further Reading

  • Justin Kan's original CS183B lecture recommends Jason Kincaid's overview of pitching press (Kincaid is a former TechCrunch reporter) for more tactical depth than the lecture had time for.
  • Trust Me, I'm Lying by Ryan Holiday, referenced in the same lecture, gives a deliberately cynical account of how stories spread online — useful context even though its author's own methods aren't this chapter's recommendation.
  • Chapter 11 covers the growth channels that take over once a company needs acquisition volume beyond what press alone can ever realistically provide.
💰

Chapter 13 — How to Raise Money

About four thousand companies try to raise venture capital in a given year. Roughly two hundred of those get funded by a top-tier firm. Of those two hundred, about fifteen will eventually reach $100 million in revenue. Those fifteen companies — out of the original four thousand — will generate somewhere around 97% of all the returns the entire venture capital industry makes that year.

Marc Andreessen, cofounder of the venture firm Andreessen Horowitz, shares that number not to be discouraging, but to explain something that otherwise looks irrational about how investors behave: venture capital is a business built entirely around finding extreme outliers, and almost every practice covered in this chapter — what investors look for, how they price risk, why they sometimes pass on companies that look perfectly reasonable — makes sense once you understand that a fund's entire return depends on finding one of those fifteen companies, not on a portfolio of merely solid ones.

💡 The Core Idea: Fundraising Is About Peeling Away Risk, One Round at a Time

Andreessen borrows a framework from investor Andy Rachleff that reframes what a funding round is actually for: think of a brand-new startup as carrying an entire onion's worth of risk, layered one on top of another. Founding-team risk — can these people even work together. Product risk — can they build the thing at all. Technical risk — does it require some breakthrough that might not happen. Launch risk. Market-acceptance risk. Revenue risk. Cost-of-sales risk, if there's a sales force. Viral-growth risk, if the model depends on one.

Each funding round exists to peel away a specific layer or two of that onion, not to provide a general cash cushion. A seed round should get you far enough to prove the founding team can execute and the product can exist. A Series A should get you far enough to prove real customers want it. Each round's size should be calibrated to the specific layers of risk it's meant to remove — not simply "as much money as investors will offer," which is a mistake this chapter returns to directly.

🔍 Why It's True: What Investors Actually Look For, and the Deals That Prove It

Strength, not the absence of weakness

The default, checkbox way to evaluate a startup goes something like: good founder, check; good idea, check; good product, check; good initial customers, check. Andreessen's observation, after years of doing this, is that checkbox deals — reasonable on every dimension — routinely lack the one thing that actually produces an outlier: an extreme, specific strength along some dimension that makes the company remarkable rather than merely solid.

The uncomfortable flip side of this: companies with a genuinely extreme strength frequently have real, visible flaws elsewhere. If a firm's policy were to avoid every company with a serious flaw, Andreessen notes, it would have passed on nearly every big winner in the firm's history. The actual discipline isn't finding flawless companies — it's finding the specific strength worth betting on, and being willing to tolerate real weaknesses alongside it, which is a much harder judgment call than working through a checklist.

Ron Conway, an angel investor who has backed several hundred companies since the 1990s, adds a specific, practical version of what he's evaluating in the first minute of any pitch: is this person a natural leader, and are they obsessively focused on the product. He's hoping the motivation traces back to a personal problem the founder actually had, not an idea manufactured to look fundable — the exact same instinct Chapter 1's "you can't not do it" test was built around, arrived at independently by an investor rather than a founder. Conway's other filter, echoing a principle this book already established in Chapter 2: a founder should be able to explain what their product does in one compelling sentence. By his own account, roughly a quarter of the founders he meets still can't clear that bar after their first sentence — and after enough years doing this, he simply stops the pitch and asks them to restart.

What investors actually check when they evaluate "revenue risk"

Two layers of Andreessen's onion — revenue risk and cost-of-sales risk — get evaluated through a specific lens worth naming directly, since it connects to a framework this book already covers in full: unit economics. An investor looking at "revenue risk" is really asking the same question Chapter 11 built a worked example around — does customer lifetime value (CLV) exceed customer acquisition cost (CAC), broken down by segment rather than blended into a single average — and an investor looking at "cost-of-sales risk" is asking whether a company's sales motion (Chapter 16 covers this directly) can scale without costing more than the revenue it brings in. A founder walking into a fundraising conversation without a clear, segment-level answer to this math is, in effect, asking an investor to take the revenue-risk layer of the onion on faith.

This is also where a real structural difference shows up between consumer software and enterprise software, worth flagging honestly: Andreessen notes that enterprise and SaaS companies frequently have no MVP-equivalent stage at all. A consumer app can launch something minimal and iterate in public; an enterprise customer, by contrast, usually won't adopt anything short of a genuinely complete product, which means an enterprise company often needs to raise five to ten million dollars just to build the first version customers will actually buy — a "cold start" with no scaled-down early version to test demand first. This isn't a reason to avoid enterprise software (Chapter 16 covers why it can still be an excellent bet) — it's a reason the onion-theory precision this chapter recommends for capital-intensive businesses applies to certain software categories too, not just to hardware and biotech.

Being fundable is easier than almost everything that follows

Andreessen's own comparison, delivered as a direct challenge to the idea that fundraising is the hardest part of building a company: raising venture capital is, in his assessment, one of the easiest things a founder will ever have to do, compared to recruiting a twentieth engineer, selling into a large enterprise, or generating real viral growth in a consumer product. His reference point is a line from comedian Steve Martin's memoir: the key to success is being so good that people can't ignore you. Applied to fundraising, that means the highest-leverage use of a founder's time is almost never refining the pitch deck — it's making the underlying business good enough that raising money becomes close to a formality.

Parker Conrad's own story, told directly in this same panel, makes the point uncomfortably concrete. Pitching an earlier company, he and his cofounder were rejected by roughly sixty different venture firms. At one of those meetings, a partner at Khosla Ventures told him bluntly, in Conrad's own retelling of the moment: if they'd been "the Twitter guys," they could have pitched anything at all and been funded on the spot; since they weren't, they needed to have all their analysis ready to justify the bet. Conrad took the opposite lesson from the one the partner intended — not "prepare better analysis," but "figure out how to actually become the kind of company that doesn't need the analysis." His next company, Zenefits, was deliberately conceived as a business that could plausibly grow without ever raising outside money at all — and that same quality, a business viable on its own, was exactly what made investors want to fund it eagerly once he did decide to raise.

Choosing an investor is closer to choosing a spouse than closing a transaction

Both Andreessen and Conway independently describe the investor relationship in the same terms: a serious startup is potentially a ten-to-twenty year project, which is longer than the average American marriage, and the investors who join the board for that stretch of time are people a founder will be relying on through real crises, not just during the good years. Andreessen's advice: weigh the choice of investor with something close to the seriousness you'd bring to choosing a life partner — not because the check size doesn't matter, but because when things go wrong (and on a long enough timeline, something always does), the difference between an investor with real domain expertise and trustworthy judgment and one who simply wrote a check becomes the whole story.

Two real investment stories from this same conversation illustrate what that difference actually looks like in practice. Ron Conway's account of investing in Google in 1999 came entirely through personal trust: a Stanford professor mentioned an early student project called "BackRub," built around a then-unproven idea — ranking search results by which pages other people linked to, rather than by keyword matching alone. Conway had to wait months and prove himself before the founders would even meet with him, and getting into the deal required bringing in Sequoia Capital first, because the founders wanted Sequoia's existing relationship with Yahoo secured before they'd accept Conway's money. Andreessen's account of Airbnb runs the opposite direction: his firm initially passed on the company entirely, missing it the first time around because — by his own admission — the idea sounded almost deliberately absurd (a website for staying in strangers' spare rooms, combined with a website where you could rent out your own spare room to strangers, which he calls "the second most stupid idea you could possibly have" layered on top of the actual most stupid one). The firm came back later, at a much higher valuation, once the numbers had clearly proven the initial read wrong — and the decision to invest heavily at that point rested as much on directly observing how mature and grounded the three founders had become under real pressure as on the growth numbers themselves.

⚠️ Common Mistakes

  • Asking investors to sign an NDA before discussing the business. Both Conway and Andreessen treat this as close to a disqualifying signal — professional investors see enough deal flow that a request like this reads as distrust rather than caution, and it damages the relationship before it starts.
  • Treating a verbal commitment as a real commitment. Conway's specific fix: as soon as an investor commits to something in a conversation, send a follow-up email confirming the details in writing immediately — investors, in his experience, frequently and genuinely forget specifics later, and a written record avoids relitigating it.
  • Raising as much money as investors will offer, rather than exactly enough to peel away the next layer of risk. More capital raised now means more dilution locked in permanently, regardless of whether that capital was actually needed for the milestones ahead.
  • Choosing an investor primarily for valuation or check size, rather than domain expertise and trust. Andreessen is direct that his own firm will walk away from an otherwise-attractive deal if a company's cap table is already too diluted or already includes investors who add no real value, because both problems demotivate the founding team in ways that outlast any single round.
  • Obsessing over the fundraising process as if it were the actual milestone. Conway's advice: treat it as one of the smallest steps in the company's life and get it over with efficiently, rather than treating a successful raise as a personal victory.

⚖️ Tradeoffs and Edge Cases

Does the onion-theory framework change for capital-intensive businesses (hardware, biotech, infrastructure)? The underlying logic stays the same, but the required precision goes up substantially. Andreessen's advice for these cases: be exact about what each round of a much longer, more expensive funding path is meant to prove, because the tolerance for wasted capital or a misjudged milestone is much lower when total capital needs stretch into the hundreds of millions across many rounds. Venture debt and lease financing become relevant options in this category in a way they rarely are for a capital-light software company — but both still require the same operational discipline this chapter's onion framework describes, arguably more of it.

What percentage of the company is reasonable to sell in a given round? There's no universal formula, but rough norms exist: a Series A commonly sells something in the twenty-to-thirty percent range; a seed round is often closer to ten to fifteen percent. Ron Conway's specific warning: think in advance about the point at which your own remaining ownership would start to feel demotivating, because a founder who's already diluted down to a very small stake after just one or two early rounds has, in a practical sense, already undermined their own long-term motivation — a problem no later success can fully undo.

Does board composition matter as much as founders often fear? Less than expected, in both Andreessen's and Parker Conrad's account, as long as trust is genuinely high. Most of an investor's real influence comes from protective provisions written into the financing terms, not from formal board votes — and in Andreessen's own two decades on startup boards, he reports never once being part of an important vote that actually came down to a close count; the real decisions get worked out through discussion and consensus well before any formal vote would matter. The exception, worth noting honestly: when a company gets into serious trouble, the terms of a prior round often get renegotiated anyway, regardless of what was originally signed — which is itself another argument for choosing investors who can be trusted to renegotiate fairly, not just investors who offered the best terms going in.

🧪 Applying the Test

Take your own company's current or hypothetical funding stage, and list the layers of Andreessen's onion in order: founding-team risk, product risk, technical risk, launch risk, market-acceptance risk, revenue risk, cost-of-sales risk, viral-growth risk. Mark which layers your last round of funding (or your current savings, if you haven't raised yet) was actually meant to peel away. Then ask honestly: is the amount of capital you're considering raising next sized to remove the next layer specifically, or is it sized to "raise as much as we can get"? If it's the second, that's worth revisiting before you start the process.

✅ Chapter Summary

  • Venture capital is a business built around extreme outliers — roughly fifteen out of four thousand companies a year generate almost all the industry's returns, which explains much of how investors actually behave.
  • Each funding round should be sized to remove a specific layer of risk (the "onion theory"), not simply to raise as much as investors are willing to offer.
  • Investors look for extreme strength, not the absence of weakness — checkbox-perfect companies frequently lack the one differentiating quality that produces an outlier.
  • Being genuinely fundable is, according to Andreessen, easier than almost everything a founder does afterward — the highest-leverage move is making the business better, not refining the pitch.
  • Choosing an investor is closer to choosing a spouse than closing a transaction — a ten-to-twenty year relationship where domain expertise and trust matter more than check size or valuation.
  • Never ask for an NDA, and always follow up investor commitments in writing immediately.
  • Board seats and formal votes matter less than expected when trust is genuinely high — most real influence runs through informal discussion and the terms written into the financing itself.

📝 Exercises

  1. Recall: Explain the "onion theory of risk" in your own words, and why it argues against simply raising as much money as investors are willing to offer.
  2. Apply: List the risk layers in Andreessen's onion framework for your own company or a hypothetical one, and identify which specific layer your next round of funding should be sized to remove.
  3. Apply: Draft the one-sentence description of your product that Ron Conway would need to hear in his first minute of a pitch, per the standard set in Chapter 2.
  4. Confront a counterexample: Andreessen's firm initially passed on Airbnb, judging it "the second most stupid idea" layered on top of an already bad one, before returning to invest heavily at a much higher valuation. What would you need to see in your own snap judgment of an unusual idea to know whether you're seeing real weakness, or just an idea that sounds bad for the reasons Chapter 2 said good ideas often do?

📚 Further Reading

  • Andy Rachleff's original writing on the "onion theory of risk," referenced by Andreessen in this lecture, develops the framework in more depth than the panel format allowed.
  • Chapter 2's discussion of ideas that "sound bad but are actually good" is worth rereading alongside this chapter's Airbnb story — the same pattern, seen this time from an investor's side of the table instead of a founder's.
  • Chapter 15 covers the legal and financial mechanics (cap tables, vesting, SAFEs) that turn this chapter's strategic advice into an actual signed term sheet.
🎤

Chapter 14 — Pitching and Fundraising Mechanics

Y Combinator ran a mock pitch exercise once, live, in front of a class. A founder played himself. A partner played an investor. The founder described his company as "a communication platform that will allow businesses and consumers to collaborate on one single platform." Asked what the product actually does, he circled the same sentence again, slightly reworded. Asked about traction, he said he didn't want to "open the kimono." Asked about the business model, he said they hadn't precisely figured it out yet, but were thinking maybe a monthly fee, they weren't sure how much. The investor ended the meeting politely and moved on.

Then they ran it again, with a second, better-prepared founder pitching a similar kind of idea. This one opened with something concrete: "when you walk into a Crate and Barrel, you can send the manager a message like, 'there's puke in the hallway.'" Pressed on traction, he gave real numbers without flinching: 350 locations, growing 11% a week, three months in. Pressed on what he'd learned, he offered something the investor didn't already know: more than half the messages sent through the product weren't feedback at all — they were simple questions, like "when do you open?" Asked about the business model, he said plainly that they didn't have a fully settled answer yet, and explained the two paths they were actually testing. At the end, he made a specific, real ask: a defined amount, on defined terms, with part of the round already committed by named investors, closing on a specific date.

Same format, same amount of time, two completely different outcomes. This chapter is about the difference between those two pitches, and about the mechanics of fundraising that surround the pitch itself — when to start, how to get the meeting, and what happens after.

💡 The Core Idea: Thirty Seconds, Then Two Minutes, Nothing Longer

Michael Seibel, a YC partner who has founded two companies himself, argues that a founder needs exactly two versions of their pitch memorized: one that takes thirty seconds, and one that takes two minutes. Nothing longer is actually necessary, and longer pitches tend to hurt more than they help — every additional minute of talking is another chance to say something an investor doesn't like, with no offsetting benefit.

The thirty-second pitch is three plain sentences: what the company does, how big the market is, and how much traction exists so far. The two-minute pitch adds four more components on top of that foundation: a specific insight nobody else has, how the company makes money, who the team is, and — only in this longer version — the actual ask. Everything in this chapter is really just an elaboration of these seven pieces, and the discipline of keeping each one to a sentence or two is not a stylistic preference. It forces a founder to know, with total clarity, what actually matters about their own company.

🔍 Why It's True: What Each Sentence Has to Do, and Why the Mechanics Matter as Much as the Words

The thirty-second pitch: what, how big, how fast

The first sentence has to describe the product in language so simple that a non-technical relative would understand it — Seibel's own name for this is the "mom test," a direct echo of the one-sentence clarity rule Chapter 2 already established for evaluating ideas in the first place. If the honest, simple version of the sentence sounds underwhelming, that's still better than a longer, vaguer sentence that sounds impressive but doesn't actually explain anything — Ron Conway's complaint in Chapter 13, that a quarter of founders can't clear this bar, is describing exactly this failure.

The second sentence states the market size, aiming at a number in the billions where a credible one exists — not because the number itself proves anything, but because it tells an investor whether the company could ever become one of the outliers Chapter 13 described. The third sentence states traction: real growth numbers if they exist, or, if the company hasn't launched yet, evidence of how fast the team is moving — a launched beta within weeks of starting, for instance — since speed is the best available substitute for traction when traction doesn't exist yet.

The two-minute pitch: insight, money, team, ask

The two-minute version adds four things, each disciplined down to a sentence or two.

Unique insight is the specific thing the founder understands that the biggest players in the space either don't understand or haven't acted on — the "aha" moment an investor should feel on hearing it. If the insight produces a shrug of recognition rather than genuine surprise, it hasn't been sharpened enough yet.

How you make money should be stated plainly, using the actual, unhedged mechanism — advertising, direct sales, subscription, whatever it genuinely is — rather than a list of several possible models offered as if to hedge against being wrong. Naming several possible business models at once doesn't read as flexible. It reads as not yet having figured out the business, which is a different and worse impression to leave.

Team should mention truly impressive past accomplishments if they exist (having built something that made real money for people, for instance) and otherwise stick to concrete, checkable facts: how many founders, how many are technical, how long the team has known each other, whether everyone is committed full-time, and how the team actually met. Padding this section with unrelated academic credentials or generic descriptors tends to draw attention to what's missing rather than what's there.

The ask is the one place in the entire pitch where using precise financial jargon is correct instead of a warning sign. Everywhere else in this chapter, plain language is the goal — but stating the ask vaguely ("we're raising some money") signals a founder who hasn't done the homework, in a section where the homework is exactly what an investor is checking for. The ask needs the exact instrument (a convertible note or a SAFE), the valuation cap, the total amount being raised, and the minimum check size an investor could write. A founder who doesn't know these numbers cold isn't ready to be having this conversation yet — Chapter 15 covers what each of these terms actually means in enough depth to use them confidently.

The two live pitches, side by side

The contrast from this chapter's opening is worth breaking into its specific, transferable lessons, because each failure in the first pitch maps directly onto one of the seven components above. The weak pitch never landed a clear one-sentence description, even after several attempts — a direct failure of the "mom test." It avoided giving real numbers when asked about traction, using the phrase "don't want to open the kimono" instead of either real data or an honest "we're pre-launch." Its business model answer hedged across several unconsidered options rather than committing to an actual mechanism. Its team description was vague and unconvincing rather than concrete. And it never arrived at an actual ask at all — the meeting simply ran out of time.

The stronger pitch did the opposite at every single point. Its one-sentence description used a specific, vivid, concrete example (the Crate and Barrel "there's puke in the hallway" message) instead of an abstract category description. Its traction numbers were exact and unhesitating. Its unique insight was genuinely surprising — that most messages sent weren't complaints at all, but simple logistical questions, an insight the company had only discovered by building the product and watching real behavior, not by guessing in advance. Its business-model answer was honest about genuine uncertainty while still demonstrating that two specific paths were actively being tested, rather than a vague shrug. Its team answer included a past failure, disclosed plainly rather than hidden, and concrete facts about who the founders were and how they'd worked together. And it closed with a fully specified ask — dollar amount, instrument, valuation cap, already-committed capital from named investors, and a specific closing date — that gave the investor everything needed to actually say yes on the spot.

When to actually start, and how to set up the meetings

Investors, unsurprisingly, prefer to invest based on real traction — so the honest strategic question isn't just "am I ready," it's "how do I put myself in the strongest possible position before I start." If traction doesn't exist yet, two substitutes carry real weight: whether investors are already proactively asking to be involved (a strong signal you're already talking about the company effectively), and whether the team has already minimized how much money is actually needed to get to market — Michael Seibel's estimate is that the large majority of startups can get an initial version of a product built and launched on very little capital. The goal in either case is the same: never put yourself in a position where the pitch implicitly says "we can't do anything until you fund us." The stronger, and usually true, position is "this is moving already, with or without you" — which flips the power dynamic in the room in a way no amount of polish on the pitch itself can substitute for.

Once you're ready to start real conversations, the practical mechanics matter almost as much as the pitch content. A warm introduction — from another founder, or from a previous investor — is close to mandatory; a cold approach with no connection starts from a real disadvantage. Fundraising should run as a sprint, not a marathon: schedule as many meetings as possible in the same tight window, rather than spreading them out over months, so that offers and momentum can compound instead of going stale one at a time. A useful, slightly counterintuitive tactic for creating that clustering: when investors respond with interest, ask to meet in two or three weeks rather than immediately, citing real work you're heads-down on — this naturally bunches meetings into the same period across every investor you're talking to, and it signals, correctly, that you're not desperate for the money right now. Finally, one person on the founding team — not the whole company — should own the fundraising process, so the rest of the team can keep building instead of getting pulled into a process that, per Chapter 13, is supposed to be one of the easier and smaller parts of the company's life.

⚠️ Common Mistakes

  • Failing the "mom test" on the very first sentence. If a non-technical relative couldn't repeat back what the company does, the sentence needs more work before anything else in the pitch matters.
  • Hedging the business model across several possible options instead of stating the real one. This reads as not having figured out the business yet, not as admirable flexibility.
  • Being evasive about traction instead of giving real numbers, or an honest "pre-launch." "I don't want to open the kimono" tells an investor there's something to hide, whether or not that's true.
  • Vague deal terms in "the ask." This is the one part of the pitch where precise jargon is required, not optional — an imprecise ask signals a founder who isn't actually ready to close a deal.
  • Spreading investor meetings out over months instead of clustering them into a tight window. Fundraising momentum compounds when meetings happen close together and fades when they're spread thin.
  • Pitching from a position of needing the money to do anything at all, instead of demonstrating the company is already moving regardless of whether this particular investor says yes.

⚖️ Tradeoffs and Edge Cases

Doesn't "use precise jargon in the ask" contradict this book's general preference for plain language? No, and it's worth being explicit about why the two don't conflict. Chapters 1 and 2 argued for plain, jargon-free language when describing an idea, because jargon there usually hides a lack of clarity. The ask is a different kind of statement — a specific, checkable financial term, not a description of what a product does — and precision there signals competence rather than obscuring anything. The rule across this whole book is really one rule, not two: use the simplest language that is still fully accurate for what you're actually saying.

What if you genuinely have no traction and no way to fake speed either? This is a real, common position for a first-time, pre-launch founder, and it's worth being honest that the "how much traction" sentence is weakest exactly here. The available substitutes — team commitment (everyone full-time), evidence of speed (what's shipped in the last few weeks), and a sharp, specific "why now" (Chapter 2) — won't fully replace real traction, but they're the honest, available alternative, and overstating traction that doesn't exist is a worse option than admitting its absence plainly.

Is it ever fine to skip a formal ask and just build relationships first? Yes — plenty of real fundraising conversations start as relationship-building meetings with no immediate ask attached, particularly with investors a founder expects to approach again later. The seven-part structure in this chapter describes what a meeting where you are asking for money should contain, not a requirement for every single investor conversation a founder ever has.

🧪 Applying the Test

Write out your own thirty-second and two-minute pitches, using the exact structure from this chapter — one sentence per component, nothing longer. Then read the thirty-second version out loud to someone with no context on your company or industry. If they can't repeat back what your company does afterward, in their own words, the first sentence isn't done yet, no matter how good the rest of the pitch is.

✅ Chapter Summary

  • Memorize exactly two pitches: thirty seconds (what, market size, traction) and two minutes (adds unique insight, business model, team, and the ask).
  • Use the "mom test" for the one-sentence description — the same clarity standard Chapter 2 established for evaluating ideas in the first place.
  • State the business model plainly, using the real mechanism, rather than hedging across several possible options.
