The standard, investor-friendly-but-normal version is a 1x non-participating preference: in an exit, preferred shareholders get the larger of either (a) their original investment back, or (b) their pro-rata share of the proceeds as if they'd converted to common stock — whichever is worth more to them. Watch for two variations that shift the balance meaningfully toward investors: a preference higher than 1x (meaning investors get back more than they put in before anyone else sees a dollar), and a participating preference (meaning investors get their preference and then also participate in the remaining proceeds pro-rata, effectively double- dipping). Both are red flags worth pushing back on if they appear in an early-stage term sheet.

Option pool. Appendix A showed exactly how this affects a cap table. The clause to watch for is when the pool gets created relative to the valuation — a pool created "pre-money" (before the new investor's money, diluting existing shareholders only) is standard; be alert to language that would size the pool in a way that dilutes only the founders and not the incoming investor.

Board composition. This clause specifies who sits on the board after the round closes — commonly some mix of founders, investors, and independent members. Chapter 13 already covered why this matters less in practice than founders fear, as long as trust is high, but it's still worth reading carefully: a board composition that gives investors control of the majority of seats this early is unusual and worth understanding exactly why, if it appears.

Protective provisions. A list of specific actions the company cannot take without investor approval — commonly things like selling the company, raising future rounds on certain terms, taking on debt, or changing the size of the board. These are normal and expected. What's worth checking is whether the list is a reasonable, standard set of major decisions, or an unusually long list that would require investor sign-off on routine operational decisions.

Anti-dilution provisions. These protect an investor if the company later raises money at a lower valuation than this round (a "down round"). The standard, founder-friendlier version is broad-based weighted average — a formula that adjusts the investor's effective price modestly, accounting for how large the down round is relative to the company's overall size. The much harsher version, full ratchet, would reprice the investor's entire stake to match the new, lower price regardless of the down round's size — a term that can be devastating to founder and employee ownership if it's ever triggered, and one worth resisting in early-stage negotiations.

Pro-rata rights. Covered in Chapter 15: the right for this investor to invest again in future rounds to maintain their percentage ownership. Standard and generally reasonable, though it does mean founders bear more cumulative dilution as investors exercise it round after round.

Drag-along and tag-along rights. A drag-along clause means that if shareholders holding a specified majority agree to sell the company, remaining shareholders can be required ("dragged along") to sell too, on the same terms — this prevents a small minority from blocking an exit the rest of the company wants. A tag-along clause is the protective mirror image for smaller shareholders: if a majority holder sells their shares, minority holders get the right to sell alongside them on the same terms, rather than being left behind as a minority partner to whoever bought in.

No-shop / exclusivity period. Once you sign a term sheet, this clause commits you to not soliciting or negotiating with other investors for a specified window — commonly 30 to 60 days — while the investor completes due diligence and finalizes the legal documents. This is standard, but signing a term sheet is a real commitment, even though it isn't the final binding agreement; don't sign one you're not seriously prepared to close.

Founder vesting / re-vesting. Occasionally, an investor's term sheet will ask founders to restart their vesting schedule (or vest a portion of previously-vested shares again) as part of the round, specifically to ensure founders stay motivated and committed for the new round's investment horizon. This is worth understanding clearly before agreeing to it, since it directly affects what happens to a founder's equity if they leave the company after this round closes.

The One Habit Worth Building

Every clause in this appendix answers the same underlying question from a different angle: what happens if things go well, and what happens if they don't. Read every term sheet clause twice — once assuming the company is a huge success, and once assuming it struggles or gets sold for less than everyone hoped. A clause that looks harmless in the success scenario can look very different in the second one, and that's exactly the scenario a term sheet's protective language is usually written for.

🧮

Appendix C — Unit Economics Formula Sheet

A quick-reference sheet for the formulas introduced across this book, gathered in one place. Each entry names the chapter where the concept was first explained in full — flip back there for the reasoning and worked examples behind the formula.

Growth and Retention (Chapters 10–11)

Retention rate — the percentage of a cohort still active after a given time period. Plot it against time since acquisition to get a retention curve; an asymptotic (flattening) curve signals real product-market fit.

North Star metric — not a formula, but a discipline: the single number (monthly active users, nights booked, messages sent) the whole company optimizes toward.

Viral coefficient (K-factor)

K = payload (invites sent per user) × conversion rate (of those invited who sign up) × frequency (how often the invite cycle repeats)

K above 1 means a user base grows on its own from virality alone; below 1, it eventually flatlines without other growth channels.

CAC (Customer Acquisition Cost)

CAC = Cost per click ÷ Conversion rate

CLV / LTV (Customer Lifetime Value) — the total net revenue a customer generates over the time they remain a customer, typically calculated over a fixed window (a "12-month CLV," for instance).

Unit economics profit per customer

Profit = CLV − CAC

A positive number means the channel is worth scaling; a negative one means you're paying more to acquire customers than they're worth.

Payback period — how long it takes a customer's revenue to repay the CAC spent to acquire them. A 3-month payback is a conservative, safe benchmark; 12 months is riskier but sometimes reasonable for a well-capitalized company; beyond 12 months moves into territory where a company is betting its cash position on an unproven prediction.

Financial Statements and Company Health (Chapter 15)

Burn rate — net cash spent per month (expenses minus revenue).

Runway

Runway (in months) = Cash in the bank ÷ Monthly burn rate

Every founder should be able to state this number from memory.

P&L (Profit and Loss statement) — revenue minus expenses over a period of time.

Balance sheet — a snapshot, at one moment, of assets (what the company owns) minus liabilities (what it owes), equal to equity (what's left over).

Cash flow statement — actual cash moving in and out, distinct from the P&L's "profit," since revenue is counted when earned, not necessarily when the cash arrives.

Fundraising Mechanics (Chapters 13–15, Appendices A–B)

SAFE / note valuation cap conversion (post-money style)

Investor's locked-in ownership = Investment ÷ Post-money valuation cap

Pre-money vs. post-money valuation

Post-money valuation = Pre-money valuation + New money raised

New investor's ownership = New money raised ÷ Post-money valuation

Vesting schedule (standard 4-year, 1-year cliff) — 0% vested before month 12; 25% at month 12; the remaining 75% vests monthly over the next 36 months.

A Reminder Before You Use Any of These

Every formula on this page is only as useful as the segment-level detail behind it. Chapter 11 makes this point directly about CAC and CLV: a single blended average across your entire customer base can look healthy while hiding a badly unprofitable segment sitting right next to a highly profitable one. Calculate these numbers by customer segment, by channel, and by cohort — never just once, for the whole company, and call it done.

🎭

Appendix D — The Full Pitch Roleplay Transcript

Chapter 14 condensed the lessons from a live mock-pitch exercise run in the original course, led by YC partner Dalton Caldwell with a founder (referred to as Qasar) role-playing both a weak pitch and a strong one back to back. The full, unedited exchange is reproduced here, exactly as it happened, for readers who want to study the complete back-and-forth rather than the chapter's summary of its lessons.

The First Pitch (Weak)

Dalton: Qasar, I understand you're coming to pitch me today. What can you tell me about what you do?

Qasar: We're building a communication platform that will allow businesses and consumers to collaborate on one single platform rather than in the fractured state that they're in right now.

Dalton: I don't follow.

Qasar: Think about WhatsApp or Snapchat. Those are for consumers. We want to do that for businesses. What that means is we want to enable consumers to talk to businesses. That's the goal of our business, or what our startup is.

Dalton: Who uses this product? What does the product do?

Qasar: It's for consumers and businesses. A messaging product that allows consumers to send —

Dalton: Why would a consumer want to use your product?

Qasar: Because they want to message a business.

Dalton: What can you tell me about the market and the opportunity? What's the size of this company?

Qasar: Messaging companies are really big, obviously. WhatsApp sold for 19 billion dollars. Snapchat is really growing very quickly as well. We think the opportunity is very big.

Dalton: Can you tell me a little bit about your traction, your numbers? Have you given this to people yet?

Qasar: We don't want to open the kimono and go into all the details here. At a high level, we definitely have thousands of users in the Bay Area. Hundreds of businesses.

Dalton: Can you tell me who some of those businesses are?

Qasar: There's ones that you've been to. We don't really want to get too much into the details because we're still early — we're trying to stay stealth.

Dalton: Okay, well, can you tell me about what you've learned so far? What insights have you had from customers?

Qasar: Yeah, the consumers are sending messages to these businesses. And we think that's great. And these businesses are responding to the messages, and we think that's — I don't think it's obvious that would happen.

Dalton: So can you tell me about what your business model is and how —

Qasar: Yeah, so we charge businesses like a monthly rate. We haven't precisely figured out what that is. Right now we're free for the few hundred companies we're in right now, but we're looking to probably do a monthly —

Dalton: How much do you think a business would be willing to pay?

Qasar: We think certainly ten to fifteen thousand dollars a month...

Dalton: Okay. So, can you tell me a little bit about your team and who you have working on this?

Qasar: Yeah, we have five founders. Technically I am the only one who's full-time right now. We're raising money so we can get the rest of the team on board.

Dalton: Can any of the founders program?

Qasar: Yeah. One of them has a Bio PhD but he's really picked up coding. I'm a Python developer — I learned Python the hard way.

Dalton: Look at the time. Well, it's been really great meeting you. Please keep me in the loop. This sounds fantastic.

Qasar: I will send you an update.

Dalton: Just keep me in the loop as this progresses.

Qasar: I'll send you an update. [Breaking character] Great, that was awful.

The Second Pitch (Strong)

Dalton: Qasar, I understand you have a company. Can you tell me a little bit about what you guys do?

Qasar: Yes, we're a messaging product — that's kind of vague. What we allow you to do is essentially message a location. When you walk into a Crate and Barrel, you can send the manager a message like, "Hey, there's puke in the hallway." Or if you're in the airport: "I can't find this specific gate — where's the terminal for Virgin?" Or at Target: "What aisle is the shampoo in?"

Dalton: So is this a mobile app?

Qasar: On the consumer side we have an iOS and Android app, but getting consumers to download apps is obviously very difficult.

Dalton: I don't usually download an app just to send a message to Crate and Barrel.

Qasar: Most businesses have a call to action that says "text the owner directly." We tested a bunch of copy, and what works best is small print saying the messages are anonymous — that lowers the barrier to entry. The most counterintuitive thing we've learned in the launch we've had — three hundred fifty locations in the Bay Area, about three months in, roughly 11% weekly growth in participating businesses — is around whether people would actually send messages at all. We weren't sure that would work.

Dalton: Do people send messages?

Qasar: They do.

Dalton: What's the number one type of message people send?

Qasar: We originally thought this would be in-location feedback — that was the premise. What we found is more than half the messages aren't about feedback at all. People ask things like — there was a kebab stand in San Jose where messages came in asking "are you hiring?" Which is strange, because you'd think they'd just ask the owner directly. But the person walking in doesn't know who the owner is, and we do, so texting through us is actually the easier path for them.

Dalton: Okay, so it's like a suggestion box — a way to message a business?

Qasar: That's what we initially thought it was. But what we actually discovered is that over half the messages are just things like: "When do you open? When do you close?" — because that's not on Google. "Are you catering? Do you have reservations tonight?" That kind of thing.

Dalton: Okay. In terms of your traction, tell me what you guys have right now.

Qasar: Three hundred fifty businesses, San Jose to San Francisco. We sold all of them ourselves — the three of us are all technical, but we did all the sales, because we learned a lot about how these businesses actually work that way. We come from a retail background. We originally built this for large enterprise players — Starbucks, Walmart — but we realized closing those contracts wasn't realistic on our runway, so we got the product into users' hands with small businesses instead, and that's when we discovered this messaging pattern.

Dalton: That sounds interesting. It sounds like you have customers. How can this be big, though?

Qasar: In terms of numbers, we see about one and a half messages on average per location per day. That might not sound like much, but compare it to a Yelp or Google review — a business might get five or seven of those in its entire lifetime. So this is a much higher volume of engagement than what they're used to, and it's private, not public. As for how we actually make money — frankly, we don't have a fully clear answer yet. There are two paths: the small-business side, or the large-customer side. Large customers, from our retail experience, pay three to four million dollars a year for feedback tools like this — think Sears, where we came from. Small businesses, in testing, are willing to pay around fifty dollars a month. I think this can be a large business, but there are two genuinely different paths to get there.

Dalton: I can see that. Can you tell me about your distribution strategy, and a little about the team?

Qasar: On distribution — what we learned selling to small businesses is that it's genuinely hard: LTV minus CAC just doesn't work out well in that segment on its own. So we have two paths. One is going up-market, back toward Starbucks or Walmart-scale customers. The other is partnering with consumer-facing companies — Yelp, Google, Facebook.

Dalton: Have you talked with them? Are they actually going to do it?

Qasar: We've talked to Google and Facebook, and we're meeting with Apple. The idea is that every time you search for a business, there should be a message button — we want consumers in the habit of knowing they can text any business directly. That gets us broad distribution. Our real vision is to become the messaging infrastructure between consumers and businesses. If those partnerships don't materialize — if Google, Facebook, and Yelp don't want to give up that surface — we still have a real business simply selling this as a feedback tool to large players directly.

Dalton: Tell me a little about the team — we're running low on time.

Qasar: There are three of us, all technical. Mike and I started a company together before — it failed, so I don't know if that's a point in our favor or not — and Sonny was an engineer before this. We come from retail. We built everything ourselves, and we sold everything ourselves.

Dalton: Okay.

Qasar: We've already had a couple of conversations with your firm. We're raising five hundred thousand dollars on an $8.5 million convertible note. Of that, two hundred fifty thousand is already committed by [named angel investors], and [a named seed fund] is willing to fill the rest of the round. We think you and your firm could bring real value given your retail experience — is this something you'd be interested in?

Dalton: Yeah, I think this is really interesting. I'd need to talk to a couple more people on my side, but I do think this could be pretty big.

Qasar: Since we've already had a couple of conversations, we're certainly willing to meet again — we're closing the round this Friday, so I'll follow up with you before then. We'd love to have you in the round.

Dalton: Okay, sounds good — I've got to run, but thanks for that.

Reading It Cold

Notice, reading the two side by side, that neither pitch differs in format, length, or enthusiasm. Every difference traces back to the seven components Chapter 14 named: a concrete one-sentence description instead of a vague one, real numbers instead of evasion, a genuine and specific insight instead of a generic claim, an honest answer on business model instead of a hedge, a real team story instead of a thin one, and — the thing the first pitch never got to at all — an actual, fully specified ask.

📖

Glossary

Terms are listed alphabetically. Each entry names the chapter where the term is first introduced and explained in full.

83(b) election — an IRS form that must be filed within 30 days of receiving restricted stock, locking in a founder's tax basis while the company's value is still low. (Ch. 15)

Accredited investor — an investor who meets specific income or net worth thresholds, presumed sophisticated enough to bear the risk of an early-stage investment. (Ch. 15)

ACRU (Activated Confirmed Registered User) — eBay's internal metric for a user who didn't just register but actually bid on or listed an item; contrasted with a raw Confirmed Registered User (CRU). (Ch. 10)

Ammunition — in Keith Rabois's framework, people who do excellent work but need direction, as opposed to a "barrel" who can drive an idea from concept to shipped reality on their own. (Ch. 19)

Anti-dilution provision — a term sheet clause protecting an investor if the company later raises money at a lower valuation. See broad-based weighted average and full ratchet. (Appendix B)

Balance sheet — a snapshot, at one moment, of a company's assets minus liabilities, equal to equity. (Ch. 15)

Barrel — see Ammunition; the person who can drive an idea all the way to shipped reality. (Ch. 19)

Board seat — a position on a company's board of directors; Chapter 15 recommends declining requests for one unless the investor adds genuine, ongoing strategic value.

Burn rate — the net amount of cash a company spends per month, beyond what it brings in. (Ch. 15)

CAC (Customer Acquisition Cost) — the cost to acquire one paying customer, commonly calculated as cost per click divided by conversion rate. (Ch. 11)

Cap table — the record of who owns what percentage of a company, and how that changes across financing rounds. (Ch. 15; full worked example in Appendix A)

Champion — a customer who actively advocates for a product to others, as distinguished from a merely satisfied customer. (Ch. 9)

Churn — the rate at which existing customers stop using a product or stop paying for it. (Ch. 8, Ch. 10)

CLV / LTV (Customer Lifetime Value) — the total net revenue a customer generates over a defined period as a customer. (Ch. 11)

Cofounder vesting — see Vesting; applies to founders exactly as it does to employees. (Ch. 4, Ch. 15)

Cohort — a group of users who joined or signed up during the same period, tracked together to see how their behavior (especially retention) changes over time. (Ch. 7, Ch. 10)

Concentric expansion — the strategy of dominating a small market completely, then expanding outward into progressively larger, adjacent markets. Introduced independently by three separate lectures in this book (Chapters 2, 3, and 16). (Ch. 2)

Contractor vs. employee — a legal distinction with different tax treatment (1099 vs. W-2) and different obligations (workers' compensation, work-hour control). (Ch. 15)

Convertible note — a debt instrument that converts into equity at a future priced round, an alternative to a SAFE. (Ch. 15)

Drag-along / tag-along rights — term sheet clauses governing what happens to minority shareholders when a majority decides to sell the company. (Appendix B)

Elephants and rabbits — Christoph Janz's framework for categorizing a business by customer count and price point — a few customers at a high price ("elephants") versus many customers at a low price ("rabbits"), with a spectrum of combinations in between — each requiring a genuinely different sales motion. (Ch. 16)

FF stock — a special class of founder stock structured so founders can sell some of it in a later financing round without distorting the common stock valuation. Setting it up at company formation reads as a bad signal to investors; the safe time is later, once the business is already working. (Ch. 21)

Four Horsemen — John Gottman's four relationship-ending behaviors (criticism, contempt, defensiveness, stonewalling), applied in this book to customer support. Stonewalling — simply not responding — is identified as the most damaging to a startup. (Ch. 8)

Full ratchet — the harshest form of anti-dilution provision, repricing an investor's entire stake to match a lower down-round price regardless of the round's size. (Appendix B)

GMV (Gross Merchandise Volume) — the total dollar value of goods or services transacted through a marketplace, as distinct from the marketplace's own revenue (its cut of that volume); eBay's GMV ran at roughly 10 times its reported revenue. (Ch. 10)

Growth accounting (new / resurrected / churned) — a framework for breaking down user growth into new signups, users who return after 30+ days away, and users who leave — useful because resurrected and churned users often dominate the numbers once a product has been around for a few years. (Ch. 10)

Honesty curve — Adora Cheung's observation that feedback gets more honest the less personally close the source is to you (strangers more honest than friends, friends more honest than family) — and that charging money for a product makes strangers' feedback even more honest still, since real money is now on the line. (Ch. 7)

Investment thesis — a written, specific hypothesis for why a company will work, including what the founder believes they know that skeptics don't; tracked over time by whether confidence in it is rising or falling. (Ch. 18)

K-factor — see Viral coefficient.

Knowledge gap — Jared Spool's concept: the distance between what a user already knows and what they'd need to know to use a product well; closed either by increasing user knowledge or decreasing what the product requires. (Ch. 8)

Last mover advantage — Peter Thiel's argument that durability, not speed to market, determines whether a company captures most of its eventual value; the goal is to be the last significant company in a category, not the first. (Ch. 3)

Liquidation preference — a term sheet clause determining who gets paid first, and how much, in an acquisition or liquidation, before remaining proceeds are split by ownership percentage. (Appendix B)

Magic moment — the specific instant a new user genuinely understands what a product is for (seeing a friend's photo on Facebook; finding the right listing on Airbnb). (Ch. 10)

Moat — a term popularized by Warren Buffett for whatever makes a business hard for a competitor to attack; in this book's terms, the characteristics that make a monopoly durable (proprietary technology, network effects, economies of scale, branding). (Ch. 3)

Monopoly (business sense) — a business with no serious competitor doing what it does, stable enough to plan years ahead and capture real profit; contrasted with perfect competition. (Ch. 3)

MVP (Minimum Viable Product) — the smallest version of a product that still genuinely solves the core problem; the emphasis belongs on "viable," not "minimum." (Ch. 7)

NPS (Net Promoter Score) — a simple 0–10 rating of how likely a user is to recommend a product to a friend, used as a fast leading indicator of retention before a full retention curve has had time to develop. (Ch. 7)

North Star metric — the single number a company chooses to align every team's decisions around (monthly active users, nights booked, messages sent), rather than running a separate, siloed growth team. (Ch. 10)

Onion theory of risk — Andy Rachleff's framework, via Marc Andreessen: a startup carries many layers of risk (team, product, technical, launch, market, revenue, cost of sales, viral growth), and each funding round should be sized to remove a specific layer, not simply to raise as much as possible. (Ch. 13)

Option pool — shares set aside for future employees, typically created or expanded at a priced financing round; where it's carved from (pre-money vs. post-money) affects who bears the dilution. (Ch. 15; Appendix A)

P&L (Profit and Loss statement) — revenue minus expenses over a period of time. (Ch. 15)

Payback period — how long it takes a customer's revenue to repay the cost of acquiring them; three months is a conservative benchmark, twelve months is a riskier upper bound. (Ch. 11)

Payload, conversion, frequency — Sean Parker's three factors determining virality: how many people a viral action reaches, what fraction convert, and how often it happens. (Ch. 11)

Perfect competition — a market with many similar sellers and no meaningful pricing power, where, per Peter Thiel, almost nobody makes real money; contrasted with monopoly. (Ch. 3)

Post-money / pre-money valuation — pre-money is what a company is considered worth before new investment; post-money is pre-money plus the new money raised. (Ch. 15; Appendix A, B)

Priced round — a financing round (typically Series A or later) that sets an actual valuation for the company, as opposed to an unpriced SAFE or convertible note. (Ch. 15)

Pro-rata rights — an investor's right to invest again in future rounds to maintain their existing ownership percentage. (Ch. 15; Appendix B)

Protective provisions — a list of major company actions that require investor approval, written into financing terms. (Appendix B)

Retention curve — a plot of what percentage of a user cohort remains active over time since acquisition; an asymptotic (flattening) curve signals product-market fit, a curve decaying to zero signals its absence. (Ch. 7, Ch. 10)

Runway — how many months of operation remain at the current burn rate before a company runs out of cash (cash in bank ÷ monthly burn). (Ch. 15)

SAFE (Simple Agreement for Future Equity) — an instrument, alongside the convertible note, that lets an investor provide cash now in exchange for equity later, once a priced round sets a valuation. (Ch. 15; Appendix A)

Support-Driven Development (SDD) — Kevin Hale's practice of having everyone on a team, including engineers and founders, personally handle customer support, to keep the product feedback loop intact. (Ch. 8)

TAM (Total Addressable Market) — the ceiling on how large a market could eventually become; useful only alongside the market's growth rate, not on its own. (Ch. 2)

Task-relevant maturity — Andy Grove's concept: how experienced a specific person is at a specific task right now, which should determine how much a manager delegates versus guides, rather than applying one uniform management style to everyone. (Ch. 19)

Term sheet — a short document laying out the proposed terms of a priced financing round, negotiated before the final legal agreements. (Ch. 15; full walkthrough in Appendix B)

Unique insight — the specific thing a founder understands that skeptics or competitors don't, required for a contrarian idea to also be a correct one. (Ch. 18)

Valuation cap — the upper limit on the valuation used to convert a SAFE or note into equity, rewarding early investors with a better effective price once a priced round sets a higher valuation. (Ch. 15; Appendix A)

Vesting — the process by which equity is earned over time rather than owned immediately; the Silicon Valley standard is four years with a one-year cliff. (Ch. 4, Ch. 15)

Viral coefficient (K-factor) — roughly, how many new users each existing user brings in; above 1 compounds into exponential growth, below 1 eventually flatlines. (Ch. 11, Ch. 17)

Why now — the discipline, credited to Sequoia, of identifying a specific, dated, external reason an idea works today and didn't work two years ago. (Ch. 2)

X and Y (value creation and capture) — Peter Thiel's framing: a company's value depends on how much value it creates (X) and what percentage of that value it captures (Y) — two independent variables, both required for a company to be genuinely valuable. (Ch. 3)

📚

Bibliography

Primary Source

Stanford University, CS183B: How to Start a Startup (Fall 2014). Twenty lectures delivered by founders, investors, and operators, taught by Sam Altman with guest lectures from Dustin Moskovitz, Paul Graham, Adora Cheung, Peter Thiel, Alex Schultz, Kevin Hale, Stanley Tang, Walker Williams, Justin Kan, Marc Andreessen, Ron Conway, Parker Conrad, Alfred Lin, Brian Chesky, Patrick Collison, John Collison, Ben Silbermann, Aaron Levie, Reid Hoffman, Keith Rabois, Ben Horowitz, Emmett Shear, Hosain Rahman, Kirsty Nathoo, Carolynn Levy, Tyler Bosmeny, Michael Seibel, and Dalton Caldwell. This book is a synthesis of all twenty lectures, reorganized and expanded; every chapter's "Further Reading" section notes which lecture or lectures it draws from.

Books

Fried, Jason, and David Heinemeier Hansson. It Doesn't Have to Be Crazy at Work. Referenced in Chapter 1 as the fullest case for the "calm company" alternative to hyper-growth culture.

Gottman, John. Referenced in Chapter 8; Gottman's research on marriage and conflict prediction is the basis for this book's customer-support framework (the "Four Horsemen," and the money/kids/sex/time categories of conflict).

Grove, Andy. High Output Management (1982). Referenced in Chapter 19 as the source of task-relevant maturity and the "measure output, not motion" framing of management.

Horowitz, Ben. Referenced in Chapter 20; Horowitz's own account of the Toussaint Louverture case draws on C.L.R. James's historical work on the Haitian Revolution.

Lencioni, Patrick. The Five Dysfunctions of a Team. Referenced in Chapter 6 as the source of the trust-conflict-commitment-accountability-results framework.

Thiel, Peter, with Blake Masters. Zero to One. Referenced in Chapters 2 and 3 as the fuller development of the monopoly-theory argument covered from Thiel's original lecture.

Treacy, Michael, and Fred Wiersema. The Discipline of Market Leaders. Referenced in Chapter 8 as the source of the best-price / best-product / best-overall-solution framework.

Walsh, Bill. The Score Takes Care of Itself. Referenced in Chapter 19 for the 49ers turnaround and the argument that operational details, consistently executed, matter more than any single strategic insight.

Essays and Articles

Andreessen, Marc. "Why Software Is Eating the World." Referenced in Chapter 2 as the fuller argument behind the "why now" discussion.

Graham, Paul. "How to Get Startup Ideas." Referenced in Chapter 2 as the source of the "live in the future" and "don't consciously search for ideas" arguments.

Graham, Paul. "Do Things That Don't Scale." Referenced in Chapter 9 as the origin of that chapter's title and additional examples.

Holiday, Ryan. Trust Me, I'm Lying. Referenced in Chapter 12 as a deliberately cynical account of how stories spread online, useful for context though not this book's recommended method.

Janz, Christoph. "Five Ways to Build a $100 Million Business." Referenced in Chapter 16 as the source of the elephants-to-rabbits customer-count/price-point framework.

Kincaid, Jason. Overview of pitching press, referenced in Chapter 12 for additional tactical depth on media relations.

The Economist. "Entrepreneurs Anonymous" (2014). Referenced in Chapter 1 as the source Moskovitz cites on founder depression as a documented phenomenon.

Other Referenced Sources

Altman, Sam. Blog post on stock option exercise windows, referenced and critiqued directly in Chapter 20.

Buffett, Warren. Shareholder letters, referenced in Chapter 3 as the origin of the "moat" terminology as applied to durable competitive advantage.

Rachleff, Andy. Original writing on the "onion theory of risk," referenced in Chapter 13 via Marc Andreessen.

Y Combinator. Standard startup legal document library (incorporation, financing, and employment templates), referenced in Chapter 15 and throughout as the practical starting point for the mechanics that chapter covers.
